V. UCONOSIC JUSTIMICAtIO1 AND RISKS
A. Prolect Justification
5.1     The Project i  aimed at building the institutional capacity and
policy environment to ensure that GOM has the capability to effectively
manage the transport sector in the next century. It is an integrated
package of sectoral policy reforms, institutional reorganization and
strengthening, manpower development, and limited investments, directed
to Initiating the long process of rehabilitation and maintenance of the
surface transport infrastructure in Hozambique. The Project, and its
envisaged successors, are justified byt (i) the contribution they will
make to the overall performance of the economy through: (a) a reduction
in the costs of transport over the entire farm-gate to market-place
network, and (b) an increase in transportation capacity over the
network, that, without the projects, would continue to be a bottleneck
to economic development; (ii) the depth and breadth of policy reforms
and institutional changes which are being undertaken by GOM, which will
ensure that the improvements are more sustainable; and (iii) the high
economic benefits expected to be generated by the individual investments
to be undertaken.
5.2     ROCS-1 is the first of three planned operations and is aimed
primarily at building the initial institutional capacity required to lay
the ground work for the projects that will follow.
S. Roads Compoents
5.3     As described in Chapter IV, the feasibility studies, final
design and preparation of tender documents for the rehabilitation of
some fourteen trunk-road sections will be financed under the ROCS-1
Project (feasibility studies for half of these are already completed).
with the actual rehabilitation works and supervision expected to be
financed under the subsequent ROCS-2 project. Technical and economic
analyses of seven of these priority sub-projects are available in the
Project file and demonstrate the economic viability of proposed
rehabilitation works (see para 5.6 for details).
5.4     Benefitss  The direct benefits from the rehabilitation and
maintenance of the priority trunk-road sections will be a reduction in
vehicle operating costs (voc)5/, which will, in turn, be translated
Into lower transport costs for the movement of agricultural products and
Inputs. Much of the rehabilitation work envisaged is, in fact, major
"deferred maintenance. If this were not to take place, deterioration
of the existing pavement infrastructure would continue to accelerate,
further increasing voc and significantly increasing the cost of future
5/      Forecasts of reduct.ons are based on common assumptions for the
vehicle operation data, cost of inputs, and a common analytical
model (Highway Design and Maintenance Standards Model (HDM).



- 60 -
rehabilitation. Ancillary benefits, from improved access and mobility,
not taken into account in the economic analysis, Includes (i) the
stimulation of production In areas which had limited access prior to
rehabilitation; (11) year-around access In areas which are now isolated
during the rainy seasons and (iii) reduced spoilage and losses caused by
lack of timely transport services. Additionally, since rural road
maintenance Is by nature labor intensive, it will generate substantial
employment opportunities and provide a steady income to many local
inhabitants. Since project works will be spread widely throughout
Mozambique, most parts of the country will benefit from the employment
generated by the Project.
5.5     Costst The economic analysis, which utilizes suitable shadc-w
pricing, includes routine and periodic maintenance costs at a level
sufficient to ensure that estimated road-user savings from improved
surfaces will indeed be realized   There is, unfortunately, considerable
uncertainty over likely rehabilitation costs because the only existing
pertinent data in Mozambique are the administrative rates used by the
1CM0Ps and some small periodic-maintenance contracts. There is little
reliable data on internationally competitive rates since there have teen
very few road contracts awarded to international firms in the country
for the last fifteen years. Base rehabilitation costs have been
estimated from limlted current local data as well as recent civil works
contracts In neighboring countries.
5.6    Sconomic Viability: Average daily traffic levels on most of the
roads under consideration are very low at present (less than 500 vpd).
This is due primarily to the security situation, which limits traffic to
daylight hours and sometimes to convoys escorted by heavily armed
soldiers, and also to current low levels of agricultural production and
the poor condition of most roads. Therefore, the most important
variable in determining the required level of rehabilitation of these
roads, and the economic viability thereof, is the assumed rate of
recovery of traffic. Without an increase in traffic levels, substantial
new investments would not be viable. In order to maximize the
likelihood that investment in road rehabilitation will at least be
viable, even if not always optimal, and to Ostretch available resources
as far as possible', it has been agreed to limit initial rehabilitation
work on the major trunk roads to the preservation of pavement and
shoulder integrity and some reduction of surface roughness. This will
be accomplished generally through heavy patching and bituminous surface
treatment, and sm drainage rehabilitation or improvement, with
reconstruction of short sections of pavement which are severely damaged.
Geomtric design standards for these roads vill be kept to the original
'as-built' standards. Only when peace returns, traffic significantly
Increases and becomes more predictable, and an effective road
maintenance organization is in place, will full SATCC road design
standarde be adopted.
5.7     Based on minimal rehabilitation works and reasonable assumptions
as to future traffic volumes, the feasibility analyses indicate that all
Project roadworks vill have an economic rate-of-return in excess of the
estimted opportunity cost of capital (122). For ease of analy3is it
has been assumed in the base case that traffic will gradvally recover to



- 61 -
historical (1975) levels within three to five years after completion of
rehabilitation, and then grow at moderate rates typical of those in the
Southern Africa sub-region. Sensitivity analyses were performed to test
the impact oft (i) variations from the basic rehabilitation costs; (ii)
variations from the initial normal-traffic volumess and (iii) the effect
of delayed opening of the rehabilitated road (not done on all sections).
The economic indicators on individual sections are satisfactory and
relevant data are shown in Annex 5-1.
Feeder Roads Program
5.8     The total length of  feeder, roads to be rehabilitated under the'
Feeder Roads Program (ERP) is expected to be at least 4,000 km in the
forty currently designated priority districts within GOM's Priority
District Program (PDP). This is estimated to be about 30 percent of the
kilometrage of secondary and tertiary (feeder) roads in these districts,
and approximately 15 percent of the national total. The first phase of
the FRP, comprising about 2,400 km in 22 districts, at a total cost of
about US$ 37 million, has been included in ROCS-1. This component is
funded entirely by various donors other than IDA.
5.9     Given the large number of individual roads and the scarcity of
relevant data, a detailed economic analysis cannot be conducted for each
segment of feeder road. Instead, a general analysis has been performed
comparing typicaV costs for feeder-road rehabilitation in Mozambique,
by labor-based methods, with typical benefits. This shows that, on
average, significant economic returns are attainable, as indicated in
the table belows
Economic Rat. of Rturne|

5.10    In addition, a few *sample* roads have been analyzed in more
detail. As an ex mple, a summary of the analysis for the fairly heavily
travelled Mbaize-Romoine Romd section (25km), which has a base case ERR
of 174Z is shown in Annex 5-1.
5.11    Experience from other countries has shown that rehabilitation
works of the kind envisaged in this project generally generate rates of
return of at least 302 to 40S. provided that 'appropriate' investments
are made and some form of screening of projects is employed. To ensure
that rehabilitation work will be economically viable, it has been
agreed to use standard criteria to screen the selection of specific
roads to be rehabilitated, within the priority districts designated by
the GON, based on factors related to anticipated costs and benefits.
These selection criteria are set out in Annex 4-5.

C. Coastal Shiln4ina Coiamemts
5.12    Most of the coastal shipping investments included in the ROCS-1
Project will be implemented either through the credit line or through
the private sector. As described in Chapter IV. the credit line will
include a requirement that a full financial analysis be provided for all
projects, and that all projects of over US$250,000 will have a rate of
return of greater than 122. Since the private sector will be repaying
the loans associated with these investments, it is believed that the
resources will be prudently invested. Furthermore, a preliminary
analysis of the major investments (available in the project file)
reveals that there are sufficient investments that generate a
satisfactory ERR to justify the credit line. A summary of this
analysis, port-by-port and investment-by-investment, is shown in Annex
5-2.
5.13    Benefits  The economic benefits of port investments and
improvements fall into three main categories:
ii)   Reduction in ship's stay time in the ports
(ii) reduction in damage and pilferage to goods; and
(iiI) net value of increased production.
5.14   Cost:s  The preliminary economic analysis includes the costs of:
(i) investments in infrastructure and equipment; (ii) maintenance of new
infrastructure and equipment; (iii) changes in operational costs; and
(iv) other improvement costs (administration, technical assistance).
All cost and benefits were calculated in real terms in US dollars.
Economic cost were used, which, inter alia, means that: (i) cost of
local labor is evaluatvjd at 302 of its financial costs; and (ii) a
ashadow exchange rate of 2,00OHt - US$l was used. The analysis was
conducted one item (or package of items if they need to be regarded
jointly) at a time to ensure that each incremental item was economically
justifiable.
D. Technical Assistance and Training
5.15   Technical assistance and training are expected to have a
generally beneficial effect on all roads and coastal shipping
activities, but the quantitative benefits have not been determined. At a
most fundamental level, without the technical assistance and training
being provided under this project, the implementation of a future
investment and maintenance program will be difficult, if not impossible.
The net benefits from this future program will outweigh the costs of the
technical assistance and training provided under this project. As can
be seen in Annex 5-1, rehabilitation of the seven road sections for
which analysis has been completed shows an aggregate net present value
(at 122) of about $65 million, roughly equal to the estimated cost of
all the institutional support to be provided under this project,
including coastal shipping.



- 63 -
5.16   The policy changes to be implemented with technical assistance
and training under the Project should help to mobilize some US$10
million to US$30 million of additional (largely private) capital in the
coastal shipplng sector, regardless of the ultimate structure of the
sector. In addition, reform of the Government's role in the trucking
industry could encourage additional donor and private funding of at
least $30 million in that industry. Furthermore, it is believed that
the awarding of concessions for the management of mall ports could lead
to an increase in productivity of at least 15-20S over current levels.
5.17   It is recognized that the technical assistance proposed is
expensive, and should be phased out as rapidly as GOM agencies acquire
the ability to plan and monitor the execution of future programs in
these sub-sectors without such assistance. Hence there is great
emphasis on training, to minimize the time during which MHozambique will
need to depend on expensive foreign technical assistance.
R. Impact on Areas of Special Attention
Poverty Reduction
5.18   Poverty reduction in rural areas will be achieved through:
(i)   increased access to markets, at lower cost, and hence
higher incomes for farmers. Average rural farm incomes
are expected to increase as a result of the Project.
Estimated increases in rural per-family income is one of
the key criteria for selection of feeder road sub-
projects. To the fullest practicable extent, local
consultation and involvement in feeder road selection will
be encouraged;
(ii) the generation of about 16,800 full-time equivalent
unskilled rural iobs on labor-based feeder road
rehabilitation and about 1,000 full-time road maintenance
jobs as lengtbmen. The ease with which crews have been
recruited for labor-based feeder road rehabilitation and
maintenance pilot projects undertaken in Mozambique with
the support of UNDP/ILO and GTZ, and the length of waiting
lists for positions on crews, are indications of the level
of existing unemployment and the extent to which this
project will provide much needed employment. Given the
need to find employment for demobilized soldiers, this
employment could contribute to the enhancement of
political stability in Mozambique. Guidelines based on
successful pilot projects in-country will be followed for
the size, composition and eqi;lpping of crews, and
recruitment and remuneration of crew members.
Remuneration will be at the official minimum wage. All
work will be on a task basis, allowing workers adequate
time to attend to other activities such as farming. Crew
formation will be done locally to enhance the feeling of
ownership of the YRP. Crew numbers, composition and



- 64 -
accomplishments will be monitored during supervision of
the Project; and
(iii) the support that the ROCS projects will give to the
overall growth of the Mozambican economy, and the
availability of lower cost produce in urban markets.
Role of Women and Other Traditionally Disadvantaged Groups
5.19   The labor-based, feeder-road pilot projects have overcome
initial shyness and have increased the employment of women on feeder-
road rehabilitation and maintenance crews to about 1SZ. This item will
be included in the reporting requirements of the Project and will be
monitored during supervision. Guidelines for the participation of women
in road works are included in the Project file.
5.20   Forty percent of the participants in the pilot labor-based
feeder roads projects are Ideslocadosl (displaced persons due to war and
famine) and many more are returning miners. The ROCS projects will also
provide jobs for demobilized army personnel in the future.
Environmental Impact
5.21   The Project involves rehabilitation of feeder roads and small
coastal ports. Its successors will involve increasing amounts of road
and port rehabilitation and, consequently, major civil works including
some earthwork which may have a negative impact on the environment,
particularly on the coastal areas. Therefore, ROCS-1 and its successor,
ROCS-2, have been classified as Category B projects from the
environmental standpoint and all feasibility studies include an impact
assessment and recommended design and construction measures to prevent
eventual negative impacts from disposal of channel dredging materials,
road excavation and borrow operations, coastal erosion, etc.
5.22   Environmental guidelines for port and road rehabilitation and
maintenance were agreed at negotiations. Application of these
guidelines will be compulsory for all road and port investments of over
US$1 million, and environmental reporto will form part of the annual
reporting requirements (para 6.2 v).
5.23   The environmental issues identified in relation to the feeder
roads and small ports components of ROCS-1 include direct and indirect
negative impacts. Expected direct impacts would result primarily from
civil works and other construction-related activities. The indirect
impacts would result if improving transport and shipping efficiency were
to promote non-sustainable exploitation of valuable or fragile
resources, leading to resources depletion and degradation of important
ecosystems. The envlronmental impact studies presented for both coastal
shipping and roads lead to the conclusion that the direct negative
impacts should be negligible at the proposed levels of rehabilitation
and/or improvement. The indirect impacts are more pertinent to both
components, but are difficult to estimate vith the level of information
available. ROCS-1 and subsequent projects, nonetheless, would include
the necessary provisions to counteract or mitigate both direct and



- 65 -
Indirect Ispacts, as part of th  Einal design process.  Annex 5-3
presents key conclusions and recomendations on the environmental Lssues
for both roads and coastal shipping.
5.24   With due care, road and small ports rehabilitation and/or
improvement will contribute to improvlng envLroonmtal conditions within
specific areas, and help increase environmental awareness of GOM and the
affected populations.
The Private Sector
5.25    The reform of the transport sector in Mozamblque will include a
number of initiatives to increase the patticlpatLon of the private
sector (do estLc and forelgn) in civll works for rehabilitation and
maintenance, port operations, and direct investment in shipping and
trucking.
5.26   Through contracting out of the major civil works, the capacity
of the nascent prlvate construction Industry wILl be greatly enhanced.
TechnLcal assistance to ensure that such contracting is performed
efficiently, and the strengthening of management system and procedures
wLthin DNIP, wlll further strengthen the private sector. Furthermore,
finacing is included in the Project to train local road contractors
(LRC) in the necessary business and managemnt skLils, and GOH lntends
to gradually transfer the mnagement of its road equipment fleet
(currently with the  CMHEPs) to the private sector.
5.27    The proposed 'road transport industry development program" will
target private-sector advancement through:
(i)   developing guLdelines for shifting the balance of the
trucking industry from the public to the prlvate sector,
including assessing the financing needs, procedures and
Intermediaries to effect privatLzatLon. VarLous
alternatlves to outrlght prlvatlzation will be examlned,
from full scale privatization of parastatal fleets, at one
extreme, to sub-contracting maagement, maintennce and
training functions to private-sector organizations, on the
other extreme (including leasing and concessions); and
(ii)  developing guidelines for the liberalization of
regulatLons and other current restrictions on private-
sector road transport actlvLtLes, including the
development of cost-based tariffs and regulatory
guldelines to encourage transport growth, whlle keeping
GO involvement in road transport actlvLtLes to a mlnium.
5.28    The development of a more efflclent coastal shipping industry is
predlcated on a more substantLal role for the prlvate sector. The
techaLcal assistance provided in thLs project will help to facllitate
prlvate-sector partlclpation in coastal shipping and encourage the entry
into the market of new private-sector enterprises, as demand warrants.

5.29   Port ooerations will effectively be transferred to the private
sector under the Project through the award of concessions for the
management of the small ports.
5.30   Technical assistance will be provided under the Project to the
commercial banks, to facilitate implementation of the credit line. This
will have an impact on increasina the role of the private sector beyond
the scope of this proiect.
F. Prolect Risks
5.31   There are significant risks associated with the Project,
relating to three broad areas (i) the difficult security situation;
(ii) the weak institutional capacity in Mozambique; and (iii)
competition for both local and foreign financing.
Security
5.32   The main risk stems from the difficult security situation in
Mozambique. The first element is the possibility that field studies for
final design of road rehabilitation, and subsequently the actual civil
works, will come under attack. Experience has shown that, with well-
planned military support, these risks can be minimized. Other donors
have managed this risk by financing GOM security forces which protect
the campsites from being attacked. However, possible disruptions are to
be expected. In addition, selection of roads to be rehabilitated in any
given period will depend in part on the fluid security situation.
5.33   The second implication of the insecurity is the impact that it
will have on agricultural recovery. Depending on how peace develops,
the rate and pattern of agricultural recovery may differ, and hence the
need for transport services in particular areas may vary. To overcome
this risk the project has the built-in flexibility for making
investments, such as through the selection criteria for feeder roads to
be rehabilitated, and the use of the credit line to finance small port
investments.
5.34   Furthermore, as set out in Chapter I, if the transport
infrastructure is not rehabilitated, then the likelihood for peace is
more remote. In any case, the physical investments recommended to be
made are valid in both war and peace, and the policy reforms and
institutional development included in this project are essential
building blocks for restoring the prosperity of the country. Since
building this institutional capacity is a long term process, the sooner
the process starts the better.
Institutional Capacity
5.35   There are risks that Implementation of the Project will be
delayed by the existing weak institutional capacity within Mozambique.
Indeed, it is these institutional weaknesses that the Project is
explicitly designed to address. The key risk elements in this area are:



- 67 -
(i)   delays stemoing from inability to adhere to agreed
grocurement schedules due to administrative-financial
bottlenecks in the GOM instLtutions involved vith the
project and in the Banco de Mocambique. Policy changes
aimed at reducing these risks have been built into the
Project. Significant *implementation technical
assistance wili be required as conditions of
effectiveness or of disbursement (paras 6.3 and 6.4);
(ii) delays in the implementation of policy reform. Although
GOM is committed to reform, it lacks the analytical
capacity to readily set out the options for reform and to
implement them. Significant technical assistance has been
included and a large degree of IDA supervision is
envisaged to assist GOH in this area. The other risk is
that, in the short term, political uncertainty will lead
to a decision making paralysis in which no one is willing
or able to make difficult decisions. Covenants will be
included in the DCA to increase the incentives for
implementing the most critical policy reforms required;
(iii) lack of maintenance equipment and qualified manpower. To
minimize this, maintenance of rehabilitated roads during
the life of the Project will be performed by contract,
with annual work programs being agreed and paid for on the
basis of unit prices for actual quantities of work
performed.  To attract regional contractors, appropriate
long-term contracts (up to five years) will be designed
for maintenance of trunk roads;
(iv) lack of suitably qualified staff to train may hinder the
training program and retaining trained staff within the
government may be difficult. The proposed capacity
building program, and the emphasis on basic training for
both roads and shipping staff, should improve the basic
skills of staff to be trained in technical areas.
Ensuring that training will lead to recognized diplomas
and to greater chance for advancement will help to retain
good staff, but in any case, provided that the retrained
staff remain in the country, training will not be lost to
Mozambique even if trained staff leave the Government.
5.36    The Project includes significant technical assistance
components. Given the poor long-term results of many technical
assistance programs in Africa, there is some risk that these components
will not meet their objectives. However, project design has taken full
account of experiences under TA projects in Africa as described in the
recent OED Report No. 8573: Free-standing Technical Assistance for
Institutional Development in Sub-Saharan Africa, as is shown in the
table belows

Fundint
5.37   The Project, and subsequent ROCS projects, are a multi-year
endeavor. To be fully successful, they require on-going funding for the
capacity building components and for the physical investments for many
years to come, both from local resources and from international donors.
In particular:
(C)   since the institutional and human resource development
technical as3istance covered under this project is only
the first phase of a proposed 15-year manpower development
program for the transport sector, adequate resources will
have to be dedicated to this sector after the projects are
completed in order for the desired results to be achieved;
(ii) once peace returns, competing priorities may result in the
availability of matching local funds (GOM contribution)
being limited and, hence, delaying implementation of some
rehabilitation or improvement. Donors will need to stand
ready to finance additional local expenditures, if
required; and
(iii) both before and after peace, significant donor funding
will be required for the road sub-sector. The risk to
overcome is that some donors might either reduce their
comitment to Mozambique or to the transport 3ector.
5.38    Other elements of the projects which will contribute to
reduction of the financing risks are: ,i) the gradual introduction of
cost recovery into the training program; (ii) the full implementation of
the Road Maintenance Pund; (iii) the introduction of cost-based tariffs
for port operations and shipping; (iv) reforma aimed at making the
sector more attractive for the private sector; (v) the phasing out of TA
with increased emphasis on local training (over time); and (vi) an
integrated strategy to keep donors well infonred on accomplishments of
the projects (reporting and annual reviews), focussed on the critical
issues, and interested in continuing to assist Mozambique.
5.39   The DCA will include requirements that GOM cousult with IDA
prior to undertaking any road investments having an estimated cost of
over US$4 million, or any coastal shipping investments costing more than
US$2 million, which are not included in this project.

B. Conditions of Credit Effectiveness

MOZAMBIQUE
FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
MAJOR POLICY RECOUENDATIONS OF TEE TRANSPORT SECTOR REVIEW
1.        There are several key respects in which transport equipment
utillation can be enhanced, with consequential reductions both in the
demand for additional capacity and in vehicle operating costs. With
respect to state enterprises:
(i)    they should be directed increasingly to hire services
from the private sector. This would reduce the
existing severe under-utilization in the state sector,
and foster the professionalization and role of the
public and private for-hire operators;
(ii)   they should increase utilization and back-haul capacity
of their own-account fleets by providing for-hire
services on their return trips, and during their down-
times or off-peak periods;
Miii) GOM ministries should be made accountable for the
fleets of their state enterprises and should direct
them to treat the transport fleets as cost/profit
centers; and
{iv)  when the security constraint is alleviated, own-account
operators such as AGRICOM and DPCCN should be required
to phase out or significantly reduce their own fleets.
2.        With respect to the ezercise of Government controls over the
iaik@tt
Ci)   restrictive licensing should be eliminated to allow the
own-account fleets of rural traders and state
enterprises to be used for the transportation of goods
and passengers:
(Li)   registration fees and vehicle licenses should be
increased in order to encourage improved utilization of
the vehicle stock, and should be uniformly applied to
all owners, with the scale of user charges being
related to the power, size and weight of the vehicle;
and
(Lii) private transporters should be accorded the same rights
and privileges as state enterprises, particularly those
enjoyed by TRANSCAIGA.



- 76 -
AM011 I - I
Page 2 of 2
3.        The principal objective of the allocation of trucking capacity
should be to incorporate all types of vehicles, employed in the
productive and distributive sectors of the economy, into the System for
the Non-administrative Allocation of Foreign Exchange (SNAAD). There
would be two additional requirements in this caset
Mi)    only vehicle dealers certified by the Ministry of
Transport and Communications (HTC) as able to operate
or have access to an adequate maintenance network would
be eligible, and
(ii)   dealers would have to comply with MTC's provincial
quotas to ensure an equitable regional distribution.
4.        Tariff policy should be Luided by the following principles:
Mi)    for the intercity market, the introduction of total
deregulation in the short term may be premature since
there are currently insufficient private transporters
to guarantee competitions
(ii)   the fundamental principle should be to set tariffs so
that they fully cover the long-run marginal costs of
the operation plus appropriate contribution for
overhead nd profit: they should reflect both the type
of infrastructure, the trip durations, and the added
cost and risk of operating in rural areas; and
Ciii)  the tariffs should be updated regularly to reflect cost
changes and should only require the ex-post approval.
Civ)   when the number of transporters in each province is
sufficient to guarantee adequate competition, intercity
tariffs should be completely deregulated.
5.        Other policy msasures should focus on:
Ci)    Government divestiture from transport parastatals and
the creation of an enabling environment to attract more
private operators in the sector;
(ii)   a road fund to ensure that financing is provided on a
regular basis for the rehabilitation and maintenance of
the road system;
(iii)  redirecting donor attention to the road and coastal
shipping sub-sectors; and
(iv)   continuation of the transport manpower development and
training program started with the Beira Transport
Corridor Project (Credit 2065-HOZ).

MOZAMBIQUE
FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
PAST AND CURRENT PROJECTS IN MOZAMBIQUE - AND THE LESSONS LEARNED
1.    This would be the first IDA operation in Mozambique's transport
sector involving infrastructure rehabilitation and policy and regulatory
reform. However, lessons learned from the Beira Transport Corridor
Project (Credit No. 2065 MOZ), in which IDA's participation includes
capacity building and technical assistance (TA), plus procurement of
locomotives, and from the road and street rehabilitation and maintenance
component of the Urban Rehabilitation Project (Credit No. 1949 MOZ),
have been given full consideration in the design of this project.
Problems and delays in procurement of goods and services, and in
managing civil works contracts have been particularly noted. Also,
project design has taken full account of experiences under TA projects
in Africa described in the recent OED Report No. 8573: Free-standing
Technical Assistance for Institutional Development in Sub-Saharan Africa
(See Chapter V). The Africa PCR Data Base does not at this time contain
a project aimed at rehabilitation and efficiency improvement of coastal
shipping transport services, but the design of this component has
benefited from the experience of NORAD and SIDA who have been involved
in the sub-sector in Mozambique for some years now.
2.    The lessons learned from the credit line components of IDA's Small
and Medium Enterprise Development Project (Cr. 2082-MOZ) and of the
Agricultural Rehabilitation and Development Project (Cr. 2175-KOZ) have
been built into the small port infrastructure rehabilitation and
improvement of cargo handling elements of the coastal shipping component
of the ROCS-1 project.
A.    TRANSPORT SECTOR PROJECTS IN MOZAMBIQUE
Beira Corridor Project (Cr. 2065-MOZ)

railways operations in the Beira Transport Corridor during the project
period; (b) manpower development and training at all levels of CFM(C);
(c) rehabilitation and acquisition of motive power for main line and
shunting operations; (d) technical assistance for the study and
implementation of a railvay/port cost accounting system and a management
information system in CFM(C); and (e) studies and te'chnical assistance
for future investments.

MOZAMBIQUE
FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
IfO         STUDY
TEEMS OF UEPEUNCI
1.    The Government of Mozambique (GOM) has applied to the International
Development Association (IDA) for a credit for the First Roads and Coastal
Shipping (ROCS-1) Project. The principal GOM implementing agencies are the
National Directorate for Roads and Bridges (DNEP) and the National Directorate
of Maritime Affairs (DNM). To ensure prompt and effective implementation of
the project, these agencies wish to review their present procurement practices
with the aim of reflecting GOM policy and IDA guidelines in achieving economy,
efficiency and fairness in procurement. This vill require reviewing,
revising, consolidating and streamlining present procedures to ensure the most
effective utilization of available resources.
2.    The above-named implementing agencies require technical assistance from
external sources to provide guidance in the improvement of procurement
practices through development of standard bidding documents and operational
directives for procurement of goods, services and civil works. Recommended
improvements will be compatible with findings of the on-going study "Public
Procurement in Africa". To this end the GOM wishes to retain a consultant
(financed by SIDA of Sweden) with appropriate background in public procurement
organizations and practices.
Obiectives
3.    The objectives of the assignment ares
(i)   To evaluate current organizational and operational constraints on
rapid procurement by DNEP and DNK/GAPROMAR, and to estimate the
approximate annual cost to GO of such constraints;
(ii) To recommend an appropriate organization and approval process to
allow decentralized and expeditious decision making, with adequate
monitoring and audit provisions; and
(iii) To develop standard contracts, manuals and proceduras to
accelerate procurement by DNEP and DNH/GAPROMAR, and which will be
compatible with expected future improvements in general public
procurement practices in Mozambique.
Scope of Work
4.    The scope of work shall include, inter alia, the following tasks:
(i)   Review the present regulations and practices for the procurement
of goods, services and civil works by GOM agencies, and any



- 81 -
ANX 2 - 1
Page 2 of 4
related current studies, in light of estimated present and future procurement
needs of DNEP and DNMIGAPROMAR;
(ii) In consultation with all authorities concerned, develop
standardized tender documents and contracts, as well as improved
procurement guidelines and procedures, consonant with
recommendations of the World Bank/ITC study 'Publlc Procurement in
Africa. Items to be treated in the guidelines include:
(a)   organizational structure
(b)   the approval process (as simplified as posslble)
(c)   monitoring and auditing
(d)   delegatLon and authority limits
(e)   specification requirements
(f) format of tender/proposal documents, including bonds
'g)   Justification and regulations for shopping, direct
contracting and other non-competitive procurement
(h)   procedures for evaluation of tenders and proposals, contract
award and monitoring of contract performance; and
Ciii) Develop a set of standard procurement documents for goods,
services and clvil works, based on those already in use or being
developed by Ministry of Finance. Standard documents (pre-
qualLfication, tenders/proposals, evaluation of tenders and
proposals, standard contracts) will be required for 'r  following
types of procurements
(a) International Competitive Bidding (ICB) for goods
(b)   International CompetLtive Bidding (ICB) for works
(c)   Local Competitive Bidding (LCB) for works
(d)   other procurement
5.    The Consultant will develop the required documents based on standard
documents now in use by, or being developed for, the GOM. Such documents will
comply with the requirements of the major donors and be acceptable to the
relevant authorities in Mozambique; findins9 of the study "Public Procurement
in Africa' also will be considered.
Qualification and zxPerience Rewuired
6.    The selected consultant should have substantial experience in public
procurement, especially with relation to infrastructure, and in organizational
development with special reference to developing countries.

MOZAMBIQUE
FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
TERMS OF REERENNCE - EQUIPRIT EVALUATION
I. OBJECTIVES
1.1       The National Directorate of Roads and Bridges of Mozambique (DNEP)
requires an updated inventory and condition assessment of the construction and
maintenance eqtipment it has transferred to the provincial ECMEPs (road and
bridge construction and maintenance enterprises), and recomamendations as to
the serviceability of the units in the existing fleet. For that purpose, it
is seeking the services of a highly qualified individual consultant for a
period of approximately three months. The necessary services will be provided
in accordance with these TOR and the respective contract.
II   SCOP  OF SERVICES
2.        General
2.1.1     The Consultant will be expected to visit the workshops and yards
where the bulk of the equipment is located, accompanied by a counterpart
designated by DhSP who is knowledgeable of heavy equipment and familiar with
the operations of the ECHEPs. The counterpart will facilitate the field
visits of the Consultant, and will assist in the compilation of inventory and
condition data, but the responsibility for the assessment of equipment
serviceability will rest solely with the Consultant.
2.1.2     The services shall be carried out in accordance with generally
accepted standards of professional practice, following recognized equipment
management principles and practices. The scope of work of the Consultant
includes all activities necessary to accomplish the stated objectives of these
services, whether or not a specific activity is cited in the TOR.
2 2 2     Principal Duties and Responsibilities
2.2.1     The Consultant will be required to carry out the following tasks:
*       Appraise the existing "Subsistema de Equipamentog and its
implementation in all the ECKEPs; particular attention
should be paid to the current omissions and deficiencies.
*       Visit all ECMEP headquarters and major equipment depots
(security permitting), and prepare a master equipment
register, in spreadsheet format, showing fleet registration
number, type of equipment, model, manufacturer, serial
number, date of purchase and estimated price, current



- 85 -
Annex 2 - 2
Page 2 of 3
location and condition, and other pertinent information.
The master equipment register wlll not include minor support
equipment (e.g., pumps, small generators, welders and air
compressors, pedestrian compactors, etc.) but will include
all vehicles, trucks, and other units covmonly used for road
maintenance.
*       Visually inspect and make random operational checks of
approximately 75Z of construction equipment less than 10
years old, and of vehicles less than eight years old, listed
in the master register &   classify the units as being
operational, non-operational but repairable, or non-
repairable (in which cases a recommendation will be made as
to appropriate disposition of the units).
*       Estimate equipment availability percentages for each ECMEP
fleet, comparing total fleet and total fleet less non-
repairable units.
*       Prepare an estimate of parts and/or consumable supplies
required to bring the fleet availability to at least 75Z
(excluding units to be scrapped or auctioned), and an
estimate of the time needed to achieve this goal.
*       Prepare spreadsheets, based on the completed provincial
master registers, listing the total fleet by equipment type,
manufacturer, and condition, and compiling master lists
showing which fleet units are operational, which are worth
repairing, and which should be disposed of.
_       Such other analyses and recommendations as are necessary in
order to accomplish the stated objectives of these services.
2.3       Reports
2.3.1     The Consultant shall present, at the conclusion of his services, a
report clearly describing his activities under this consultancy and presenting
his principal findings, conclusions and recommendations.
2.3.2     All documents and reports developed by the Consultant and paid for
under these services shall become the property of GOM upon completion of the
services. Language of the documents and reports shall be English, except that
the master registers of equipment shall be in Portuguese. The format of the
registers and the final report shall be agreed with DNEP.
2.4       Contract Period
2.4.1      The contract period for carrying out the services specified in
these TOR shall be three calendar months, unless otherwise agreed by the
contracting parties, although it is expected that the field work will require
no more than 60 calendar days. Services under the respective contract shall

HOZAMBIQUE
FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
DEVELOPUHEU  OF A PROGROM TO IROVS REVENUE GONERATION
AND BUDGET ALLOCATION POR TSE ROAD SECTOR
DRAFT TERMS OP RXFERCNCE
Background
1.    The Government of Mozambique (GOM) is embarking on a major roads and
coastal shipping project (ROCS) aimed at supporting its Economic and Social
Recovery Program (ESRP). In addition to a large rehabilitation program
covering some 1,000 km of trunk roads and 6,000 km of regional and feeder
roads, ROCS aims to ensure that funding will be available to maintain the
road network once initial investments have been made.
2.    The total resources required to finance a priority program in the
roads sector over the next five years is expected to be about US$200
million. Of this the Government contribution is expected to be US$20
million (from the development budget) plus about US$50 million for
maintenance costs through FY1995196. Table 1 below sets out an estimate of
the local cost financing requirements for the next five years. To mobilize
the required local resources GOM has decided to increase the contribution
to be provided by road users.
Local Expenditures       1991   1992   1993    1994    1995    1996
(US$m)
Development
Reauirements
Maintenance
Reauirements
I Total Local
Requirements
3.    The resource mobilization objectives of GOK are to improve cost
recovery from road users in order to make the sector financially self-
sufficient in terms oft (a) covering the recurrent costs of road
maintenance operations; and (b) making net contributions to the.
Government's general tax revenues which would cover at least a part of the
budget requirements for road development and rehabilitation. It is not yet
clear how much of the development budget should be recovered from road
users and how large a contribution (if any) should be made by road users to
the general government budget.



- 88 -
Annex 2 - 3

5.    To ensure that the funds will be available as required, and to
allocate user fees to road maintenance activities in an efficient and
timely manner, GOM has decided to implement a Road Fund for which the
enabling legislation was approved in late 1989. However, further
refinement of the modus operandi of the road fund as well as practical
solutions for actually improving revenue generation and collection are
necessary for the program to be effective. As currently conceived the Road
Fund faces a number of difficulties and uncertainties:
a.    The furd is required to allocate 802 of the revenues on fuel
received to the rehabilitation and maintenance of city roads,
while the major requirement for funding is on rural roads.
b.    IA 1991 the road fund will be able to cover the estimated costs
of road maintenance and rehabilitation, but as requirements
increase, the fund will prove inadequate,



- 89 -
Annex 2 - 3
Page 3 of 6
requiring central government to make additional contributions
to road activities. This raises three issuess
i.    Should other road user charges (such as vehicle taxes) be
added to the road fund?
Ui.   Should Mozambique increase its taxes on fuel? How do the
taxes on fuel compare with those in neighboring
countries? and
iii.  Shorld supplemental allocations from the central budget
be routed via the Road Fund?
Issues and NsthodoloRg
6.    In view of the foregoing, the Ministrv of Finance (MOF) and the
Ministry of Construction and Water (MCA) would like to receive practical
recommendations on the following specific issues:
a.    how best to increase overall revenue intake from road
users and to ensure equitable distinction between users;
b.    now to improve the revenue collection system from road users to
minimize leakages; and
co   how to refine the operating modalities of the road fund.
7.    Increase Revenue Intake.  The consultants will investigate reasonable
options for increasing the overall intake of revenues from road users to
finance the road budget requirements. This may be through increase in
levels of charges or through the introduction of new forms of collections,
such as vehicle registration and /or sales taxes. In analyzing the
specific means to increase the revenue intake from road users, the
following key e.znents will be taken into consideration:
4.    different types of heavy commercial vehicles and passenger
vehicles should be taxed according to the extent to which they
cause road damage.
b.    the tax system proposed should also apply to all foreign
vehicles entering into Mozambique (particularly the heavy
vehicles), including provision for heavy penalties on over-
loaded vehicles entering and exiting the country (as well as on
domfestic vehicles);
c.    the tax rates should refla't the distribution of gross vehicle
loading over available axles, depending on vehicle
configuration and legal loads, taking into account the
exponential damage to roads caused by the overloading of single
axles and of a series of tandem axles;
d.    the tax should not decrease with the age of the vehicle, as
this bears no relation to the damage caused;



- 90 -
Annez 2 - 3
Page 4 of 6
e.    value-added tax, whose base increase depends on the level of
inflation, is preferable to fixed tax rates;
f.    administration of tax collection should be kept as simple as
possible vith sufficient safeguards to minimize tax evasion and
leakages.
g.    small revenue sources which barely cover the cost of
collection, or which are difficult to collect, should be
dropped. The tax on spare parts, the bulk of which unfairly
falls on private vehicle owners, also should be removed as this
would lower the cost of vehicle maintenance and rehabilitation,
and lengthen service lives, consistent with GOM economic
recovery objectives.
8.    In estimating the total revenues to be generated, funds should cover
at least the costs oft (1) Government contributions to routine and periodic
maintenance; and (2) the net contribution of road users to the general
revenues of the Government. The estimated level of revenue requirement for
each year is given on the bottom line of Table 1. As noted in the table,
the requirements will continue to increase. The proposed tax regime
should, therefore, take these into account by proposing a gradual increase
in the level of taxation, rather than a large one-time increase, and
establish a mechanism for automatic increases to cover government
objectives. The proposal for various tax levels should also take into
consideration the assumption that the volume of road traffic as well as the
importation of vehicles and spare parts will increase in line with the
forecast economic growth (4-5Z per annum GDP growth) and with improvement
in the security situation in Mozambique.
9.    Based on these assessments, propose a program to change the tax
administration and levels of taxation, on an annual basis, outlining the
actions to be taken as well as the annual targets for revenue generation
from each of the tax instruments. Additionally, the consultants will make
an estimate of the overall cost increase of end users (in percentage terms)
caused by the increase in the tax levels.
10.   Revenue Collection.  It is believed that there are considerable
leakages in the existing instruments used to raise road user revenues. For
example many exemptions are granted for Xai-Xai lBridgel such that actual
collections are about 60Z of potential revenue. At the same time the
methods for collection of tolls are believed to be less efficient and more
costly than they might be. The consultants should discuss each potential
collection instrument with officials responsible for operating these
instruments to discern practical ways to reduce the leakages and simplify
collection. The consultants will review specific causes of leakages as
well as the high rate of exemption being granted in many cases and propose
ways in which to rectify the situation. Specific time-bound actions should
be proposed by the consultants to improve the situation.

11. Road Fund. In managing the funding requirement for road maintenance,
MOF would like to consider various options for the practical implementation
and operation of the Road Fund.
12. The exact mechanism of road fund administration has yet to be fully
clarified, and the consultants are requested to propose various options to
MOF In this regard. In specific, the consultants will consider the
following Issues in analyzing the various options:
a.    the proportion of annual revenues entering the road fund to be
automatically allocated to road maintenance (e.g. 502, 80,
100 etc.) and the logic behind the proposed level;
b.    the sources of funds - revenues from which road user charges
should be deposited into the fund, should they all go into the
fund or should some go directly to the central budget;
c.    how should the fund be held (e.g. in the central bank,
commercial bank, escrow account, etc) and who would be
responsible for managing the fund;
d.   what should be the method to permit allocating to road
maintenance any remaining amount in the fund, after the
automatic allocation is made;
e.    role of MOF, the Parliament and the Ministry of Construction
and Water (MCh),including the National Directorate of Roads and
Bridges (DNEP), in managing the fund;
f.    who should be held accountable for the management and how to
control the use of the fund to minimize misuse;
s.    how should the funds be disbursed.
13.   In considering these options, it should be kept in mind that the
budgetary requirement for road maintenance will continue to increase for
the next several years and that the proposed fund should be sufficiently
flexible to take this situation into account. The consultants should
clearly spell out the pros and cons of the options being proposed, and that
a provision for adequate monitoring and control system be installed in the
system.

MnZAMBIQUE
FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
ASSESSNENS OF ROAD TRANSPORT SERVICES PERFORMANCE
1.        The review in tais annex covers road transport. Intermodal
transit logistics are covered in Annex 3-6. A. detailed review of the
sector is included in the project file.
2.        At the outset it is important to note that existing data is
scarce, not always - .liable and sometimes contradictory. There is an
overriding need for consistency in collecting and processing
information. With regard to transport services, this need will be
addressed in the proposals for an action program in Annex 4-6.
I. Cost. Operational Performmace and Options for Private Sector
Participation
3.        Trucking in Mozambique has three overtiding operational
characteristics  first, poor vehicle utilixation in terms of loaded
kilometers (an average of 30,000 km. per year at about 60Z load factor);
second, short vehicle life (between 18 and 48 months on the average);
and third, poor maintenance planning, facilities and services. In
addition, inputs to the road transport industry are in short supply
(poor availability of spare parts, as an example, is partly the result
of foreign-currency scarcity and, partly the result of proliferation of
vehicle models of disparate origins - about ninety makes and models in
total) and, when available, expensive (landed costs for vehicles and
spare parts in Mozambique are about 60X over CIF costs).
4.        As a result, vehicle operating costs are high, ranging from
120 meticais (MT) per ton/km (about US$ 0.09) for a large/medium size
truck operated by a private operator with well organized preventive
maintenance and fleet control, to 186 meticais per ton/km (USS 0.14) for
a long-distance high-capacity freight vehicle operated by a public
transport enterprise , to around 555 meticais per ton/km (US$ 0.41) for
a medium distance, medium size vehicle operated by AGRICOM. As a
comparison, vehicle operating costs for similar vehicle types in the USA
range between US$ 0.035 and 0.06 and in Pakistan between the equivalent
of US$ 0.025 and 0.04.
S.        Vehicle operating costs and fleet indicators for AGRICOM, a
public enterprise engaged ginter alia in transport and distribution of
agricultural products suggest that AGRICOM's performance is lower than
average industry performance (private operators or TRANSCARCA of Beira)
as an example; this is due to tbe operational conditions of AGRICOM's
fleet, one-third to one-half of which operates in rural areas in harsh
operating conditions. In a way, however, AGRICOM's indicators are
typical of the environment of road transport in Mozambique (poor roads,



- 94 -
Annez 2 - 4
Page 2 of 10
war hazards, real time risk of vehicle destruction, unavailable
maintenance, etc.) which is a deterrent to the growth of private sector
entrepreneurial capacity. In fact, on account of current vehicle prices
and potential high cost of operation, road transport is not an
attractive venture to investors.
6.        As a consequence, the private sector has a very minor
participation in overall capacity and the demand, a large part of which
develops in rural areas or in areas affected by war, and thus is served
by parastatals (public and own-account) with poor incentive to offer
quality services and vith poor fleet management and maintenance
practices. The hostile environment, in both physical hazards and costs,
should be given as much responsibility as the policies of the Government
of Mozambique (GOM) in discouraging the private sector.  If normal
conditions return with peace, there should be a gradual return of
entrepreneurs to private operation of transport in particular small
traders. According to various sources, no more than one hundred small
transporters offering their services for hire (other than traders)
remain in Mozambique, and those are limited because of the ages of their
vehicles, and are mostly concentrated in the south. GOM, through the
its National Directorate of Road Transport (DNRT) is now encouraging
these operators to form cooperatives and is seeking international
assistance to develop more effective support and coordination.
7.        In spite of this, the private sector shows signs of vitality.
The lowest vehicle operating costs fouad by the ROCS-1 appraisal mission
in Maputo belong to a mostly passenger transport operation. Another
efficient private operator has built up its own fleet from older
discarded vehicles bought from public enterprises, with assistance from
German rehabilitation aid programs, together with assistance and
trainink from a well known automotive supplier in Maputo.
S.        On the end of the public transport services spectrum, the
massive food aid programs underway in Mozambique, carry out substantial
expatriate-managed road transport operations and have been responsible
for bringing into the country substantial transport resources (about 300
vehicles in this services could be privatized in the foreseeable
future). Joint ventures, such as TRANSCARGA of Beira, which has enjoyed
technical assistance Yrom SIDA, are operating at above average
performance levels, and are earmarked for privatization in the future.
Other public or semi-public transport companies, such as Camionagem de
Mozambique (35 vehicles of which 25 are operational), Rodoviaria
Nacional de Passageiros (RONAP, 95 vehicles, of which 40 are
operational, and 1439 employees), Transportes Publicos de Maputo (TPM,
177 vehicles, of which 95 are operational and only 31 in service, and
1.035 mployees), have an extremely high ratio of employees per
operational vehilel (about 30/40), extremely high operating costs, and



- 95
Annm.z 2 - 4
Page 3 of 10
although earmarked for privatization 1/, it is unclear when and how
such change could take place. Often, is in the case of RONAP, the
assets of such companies are so depleted that there is little to
privatize, except dilapidated plant facilities and a few operational
vehicles.
9.        Although Mozambique has recently issued new transport
regulations21, the policies contained in such regulations represent a
continuation of former policies rather than a change.  The core o. &he
regulations is still protection to railway transport through conW       of
entry (licenuing ,and pricing (tariffs).  In specific aspects, s .  as
international transport, current policies are drastically restrictive
and will not encourage private investment even in a market like
Mozambique where about 552 of freight moved from ports is transit
freight.
10.       There is no doubt that policies in the transport sector,
including tariffs and privatization, have been influenced by the Bank's
Transport Sector Review. No significant changes have been made in spite
of that. The general impression seems to be that new policies will not
be a drastic departure from past guidelines or regulations. In other
words, entry for the private sector will not be facilitated, labor
policies will not be changed, and tariff deregulation will not be
complete. dew policies will probably emphasize that truck and spare
parts allocation will not be based on quantity allocations, will promote
the SNAAD (non-allocation system of foreign exchange), and will also
promote, at least on paper, for-hire transport. The feeling exists,
however, that there is a basic difference between the Bank's thinking on
privatization and GOM's perception of the process. For the Bank,
privatization means the sale to private parties of public assets or
parts of them. For GOM, privatization means the conversion of a
parastatal company into a company of limited responsibility, or
alternatively, the sale of a public sector company to other public or
semi-public enterprises, but not necessarily an outright sale to private
interests 3/.
11.       In light of the high inefficiency of the industry, of the
proliferation of unused and unusablq resources (vehicles in need of
1/ The Commissao Instaladora (a GOM organization dealing with
privatization of public enterprises) was requested to prepare the
privatization of public transport enterprises in July 1990.
2/ Regulamento de Transporte em Automoveis (Council of Ministers Decree
No. 24/89 of August 8, 1989).
3/ As an example, Camionagem de Mozambique could be sold to NAVINTER
and TTA (both public) as part of the privatization process, or allowed
similar corporate arrangements as TRANSCARGA.

rehabilitation) and of the entrepreneurial capacity which the private
sector seems to have preserved, more clear policies in this area are
extremely urgent.
12.       Various alternatives to direct privatizatiou (which may or may
not be feasible in light of the depleted assets of existing companies)
should be examined. Once GOM has decided to broaden the role of the
private sector in transport, there is a range of options open to it from
full scale privatization of all parastatal fleets at one extreme, to
contracting out management, maintenance and training functions and
operating concessions to private sector interests, at the other.
Whatever proportion of the parastatal fleets demand are to be met by the
private sector will depend crucially on (a) the strength of the private
sector, and (b) the willingness of GOM to allow current public sector
activities (such as priority commodities distribution and international
transport) to be carried out by private entrepreneurs in a non-hostile
policy environment. The point to remember is that a positive 0O0
attitude towards private sector involvement will be essential to the
devel6pment of cost effective and efficient transport. There are three
main reasons for this: first, conditions of employment in the public
sector tend to result in low discipline and motivation, making it
difficult to sustain a results-oriented management; second, there exists
*lready a competitive private sector with a wide range of skills, which
further development depends entirely on the future policy environment of
004; third, development of sound road transport requires equipment and
facilities through the country that no government organization can
manage and can afford at an economic cost; thus, selective use and
encouragement of private sector facilities can greatly increase the
szope and cost-effectiveness of the Mozambican vehicle fleet.
13.       Development of private sector initiative can take two formst
Mi)    where the private sector provides a substantial and
increasing part of the total demand through
liberalization of existing restricdtve policies and
financial facilitation of investments; and
(ii)    where the private sector provides specific services to
GO and parastatal fleets.
In this latter role, the private sector can be particularly effective
across a whole range of economic activities. Some of these activities
ares
*       Management by concession:  with the aim of
strengthening fleet and labor management, workshop
management and training.
*       Maintenance democratization:  aiming at
diversification and development of automotive
maintenance workshop capacity at the lower level of

through dic^ntinuing current licensing barriers and
opening up access to foreign eschange resources.
*       Spare parts supply democratization (tied up to a more
efficient SNAAD).
*       Training# by encouraging training of operators and
mechanics by the private sector through adequate
incentive systems, and by stipulating training
covenants as part of flees and workshop equipment
procurement.
II, Tariffs, Regulations and Institutional Capacity
14.         The Government has long controlled the prices of all types
of transport in Mozambique. Established tariffs are comuonly considered
insufficient to meet the real costs of road transport. Small tariff
increases have not reflected the large import content of transport
costs, a depreciating metical, declining road conditions, and increasing
security risks. The industry shortfall of tariff rates was stated at
IS0Z for the last quarter of 1990 and at 302 for the first quarter of
1991. Under realistic assumptions, the official tariff of 1989 did not
cover transport costs even with the best of road conditions. To the
extent that tariffs continue to be fixed at unrealistic levels and state
transport firms continue to be given an advantage in access to foreign
exchange and a subsidy in covering at leart part of the transport cost
differentials, it is difficult for private sector firms to compete vith
the public sector or indeed to invest at all in transpor' enterprises.
15.       Despite the fact that the Government has recently increasingly
deregulated prices in the economy, and that this trend is likely to
continue, several noteworthy aspects of the pricing system still
represent potential constraints to the healthy development of
Mozambique's domestic marketing networks. Three of such constraints in
particular are a deterrent to efficient operationst
Mi)     established margins between various stages of the
marketing process may be too narrow to encourage the
private sector to compete with subsidized state
marketing agents;
(ii)    controlled transport tariffs may be too low to permit
private traders (or transporters) to compete with
their public sector counterpartsl and
(iii)   the pan-territorlal nature of fized prices discourages
competition and indeed any private sector activity in
remote or Insecure areas.



- 98 -
Anne: 2 - 4
Page 6 of 10
16.       The margins at different levels of the agricultural
distribution system are officially and centrally fixed vithout regard
for differential costs of moving goods various distances under vastly
different road and security conditions. As private trading begins to
emerge throughout Hozambique, differential costs are reportedly being
factored into prices actually paid and received. But with the state
still the overwhelming participant in the marketing process, such
differential costs continue to be covered by government subsidies,
despite GOM's attempt to price at an average level throughout the
country. In fact, these subsidies are exaggerated because market
influences ensure that private traders leave the higher-cost marketing
functions to AGRICOM as the "buyer of last resort, while outpricing
AGRICOM in lower-cost situations. To the extent that pan-territorial
prlcing is binding, it acts to discourage private sector competition
with the more easily subsidized state marketing and transport firms. It
further acts to inhibit normal market flows from surplus to deficit
areas, since scarcities and abundances cannot be reflected in prices,
thus reducing marketing activities and, eventually, if surpluses cannot
be marketed, reducing production.
17.       USAID has made liberalization of transport prices a covenant
to their proposal to increase by US$10.0 million the assistance provided
under the ongoing Private Sector Support Program for FY1990 41.
18.       In addition to road transport tariffs, two regulations affect
delivery of transport services and would affect entry of the private
sector in trucking operations. These are the Automotive Transport
Regulation (Regulamento de Transporte em Automoveis), Decree 24/89 of
August 8, 1989. and Law No. 7/79 of July 3, 1979 regulating comnercial
activities by the private sector.
19.       The Automotive Transport Regulation has been referred to in
paragraph 10 above. It substitutes former regulation No. 2055 of
January 7, 1961, but does not introduce modern and flexible channels to
develop sound and efficient transport services. In the light of GOM's
plans to improve transport services, the current regulation should be
reviewed.
20.       The law regulating private sector commercial activities dates
from 1979, does not provide an adequate policy for development of strong
private sector participation in transport activities and should also be
a candidate for review. Updating of this law vould be a priority.
21.       In conclusion, bringing transport economic, administrative and
operational regulation to modern and cost-effective standards, is a
4/ The other covenants are: liberalization of agricultural pricing
policies, and to open to the private sector the sales and services
networks for providing inputs through the country (USAID, PSSP amendment
656-0208 and 656-0218).



- 99 -
Anne: 2 - 4
Page 7 of 10
priority. In spite of the fact that costs produced by the industry in
support of tariff increases may reflect a high percentage of
inefficiency 51.
22.       Finally, the institutional responsibility of road transport
operations at GOM level, needs strengthening. DNTR shares control of
industry operations with the Vehicle Services Department of the City of
Maputo's Transport and Traffic Directorate (DTT) for licensing of
vehicles and drivers, and with the National Police for control of
overloading. There is no evidence of planning, and management appears
to be inexperienced, appoin 3d for political reasons, and lacking
professional background. L. a result, essential activities such as
traffic inspections, administration and licensing, driver testing, and
control of overloading are not professionally managed or discharged.
23.       At the institutional level, the two most important needs are
by far vocational training and professional motivation.
24.       One direct consequence of weak institutional capacity is that
Mozambique has a poor traffic safety record. The high accident rate and
the high mortality rate clearly show that there is a serious road safety
problem in Mozambique. Research in other developing countries indicates
that the real cost of road accidents to the society as a whole is
somewhat like 2-3 percent of GNP or may be even higher. With a GNP of
approximately 160 billion Meticais in 1986, the cost of road accidents
in Hozambique can be estimated to be some 3-4 billion Meticais a year.
An important part of this sum represents repair to, or replacement of
vehicles. This is paid in foreign currency, which is badly needed for
better purposes.
25.      The Department of Road Traffic of DNTR is the office for
vehicle control and vehicle registration. In each of the 10 provinces
there is an ezecutive Road Traffic Office. Vehicles are, in principle,
Inspected when they are brought into the country for permanent use in
Mozambique. There is no technical equipment for vehicle control and no
experienced manpower for this task, and the inspection is therefore
purely visual. If the vehicle is considered not to be in running
condition, the owner may be asked to get it repaired before
registration. The earlier practice of biannual control of vehicles is
no longer followed up. The police is undertaking some random inspection
on the road, but this is also purely visual. The present lack of spare
parts calls for a certain indulgence from the police. All vehicle
registers are kept manually and there are no data available on vehicle
Sl If Mozambique operators could reach the productivity levels of
Zimbabwean operations (100.00 to 200.000 km per year at 75Z load
factors) current tariffs would appear reasonable. Even at current
private sector operational levels (paragraph 5) current tariffs would
cover costs (excluding depreciation of replacement costs at current
prices).



_ 100 -
Annex 2 - 4
Page 8 of 10
control and the technical standard of the vehicle fleet. The quality of
the fleet is poor and some of the units are in very poor shape. There
is also a considerable passenger transport in unauthorized vehicles,
reflecting the great need for transport and the lack of public transport
facilities.
26.       Driver training and examination is also the responsibility of
the Department of Road Traffic vithin DNTR. There is one public Driving
School in each province plus one in the City of Haputo. The one in
Haputo is the biggest school, with 19 instructors and 600 candidates a
year. Six years of primary and secondary school plus a driving license
are requLred to work as a driver instructor. Most of the instructors in
Maputo have experience from before independence and there is no
structure for training new instructors or examiners. The Institute for
Driver Instruction in Harare, being open also for candidates from other
SADCC countries, has never been used by Mozambique.
27.       Overloading is a problem in all the countries in the region,
in particular on the most mountainous parts of the road network. Heavy
overloading is often combined with poor braking systems and, in this
respect, Mozambique is certainly no exception. Based on a regional
problem description, a proposal to harmonized legislation and control
system has been prepared by SATCC. Subsequently, a National Project on
weighbridges has been prepared for Mozambique.
III. Vehicle Resources. Maintenance Capacity and Foreitn Exchanae
Allocation
28.       Details of Mozambique's vehicle fleet vary considerably.  The
latest estimates can be traced to the Bank's Transport Sector Review
based on 1987 data. More recent estimates of operational vehicles
(possibly 1988) appear in SATCC, Mozambique's Road Safety Project
prepared by the Institute of Transport Economics of Oslo, Norway (Table
10). DNTR and USAID place the number of vehicles today at nearly 50.000
with about 202 heavy vehicles and a very depleted bus population of
about 2.5 (including minibuses).
29.       These figures, however, could be misleading.  The companies
visited and those institutions for which information was available
(CARE, DPCCN) represent a total ownership of about 1.800 heavy vehicles
and buses, a substantial 20S of the heavy vehicle and passenger bus
fleets in the country. These companies have reported averages of about
50 to 60S operational vehicles in their fleets. Of these operational
vehicles, however, only about 60 to 70Z were available for service. A
visual inspection of vehicle yards and workshops indicated that a high
percentage of the vehicles shown as operational were in long-term
unavailability due to lack of spare parts or major components.
Unavailability periods might last for years. If such sltuation is
accounted for, the vehicle fleet available for service in Mozambique
could be drastically reduced.



- 101 -
Annex 2 - 4
Page 9 of 10
30.       As Di other African countries, the diversification of the
fleet (about 90 different makes and models represented among the
companies visited), makes provision of spare parts and components and
expensive and difficult undertaking.  Indiscroiunate Ifport of vehicles
by donor agencies and government institutions without adequate spare
part supply quotas (many from Eastern Europe which are now obsolete)
seems to be at the origin of the problem.
31.       In spite of the critical situation of the fleet, however, the
estimates for vehicle replacement prepared by donor agencies such as
USAID, or by AGRICOn and DPCC, do not seem to take into account the
potential for rehabilitation of existing resources already in the
country.  These resources are substantial and valuable and should yield
a high percentage of vehicles suitable for rehabilitation at an economic
cost. Preliminary estimates show that about one-third of the vehicles
in non-operational condition could be rehabilitated at a cost of less
than US$15.000 (excluding vehicles of over 5 years of age) 6/. An
inventory of current vehicle resources and needs for rehabilitation
and/or replacement seems essential.
32.       Haintenance capacity in Mozambique is concentrated in few
concessionary companies located in the main cities (in particular
Maputo), with no significant network (ENTREPOSTO Comercial de Mozambique
is an exception). Most operators vith ten vehicles and over, and all
parastatal companies are, therefore, obliged to have their own
maintenance facilities, which in the case of most parastatals are
inefficiently managed. On the other hand, the few Import
concessionaires operate in oligopoly conditions and their prices reflect
these conditions. As a result of the scarcity of facilities for
maintenance, of the high cost and scarcity of spare parts (related to
poor foreign currency allocation systems), and of the continuous imports
of new vehicles into the country (mostly by donor agencies or with
specific bilateral credits), there exists no motivation to repair, in
particular at the parastatal level.
33.       If Mozambique is to upgrade its transport industry to cost
effective levels, it is essential to open up the current restrictive
workshop capacity environment by deregulating lt first, and by
effectively encouraging development of a-equate workshop capacity at the
lowest level. Three measures seem essential and should be tied up for
decision-making purposes:
lFirst, deregulativn of those aspects of workshop licensing
which are related to installation of workshop capacity for
foreign vehicles and authorizations for Imports of spare parts
and components from foreign suppliers. This measure should go
a long way in encouraging private sector and small
entrepreneur access to work shop capacity.
6/Eight to te'J percent of replacement cost (Table 2).



- 102 -
Annex   2 - 4
Page 10 of 10
Second, facilitating foreign exchange allocatlon for workshop
equlpment spare parts and components at all levels related to
automotive maintenance and by speciflcally provLding a
mechanism for such facilitation within the SNAAD.
Th'rd, encouraging development of workshop capacLty at the
provincial level.
34.       A recent report from USAID suggests that the *non
admlinitrative allocation of foreign exchange' (SNAAD) does not operate
sufficLently well. Procedures in the SNAAD are not less cumbersome than
those under any other administratlve allocation system (the process of
obtainLig forelgn exchange unde- the SNAAD takes over 200 days) and
access to the SNAAD is barred to importers who wish to compete with
manufacturer-authorlzed comodity dealerships llcensed in Mozambiaue.
WLth a parallel market rate now running at about twice the level of the
official exchange rate, there is little doubt that the metical is still
sLgnificantly overvalued at its official level. Consequently, the
establishment of the 'secondary foreign exchange market' in October 1990
was an important move. The problem now facing Mozamblque on the
exchange rate management side is how to ensure that the SNAAD and the
secondary market, as two separate experiments in market-based foreign
exchange allocation, can become complementary. The future decisions on
the foreLgn exchange policy environment in Mozamblque wlll be cruclal to
the establishment of a strong workshop capacity with adequate supply of
foreign inputs.
