FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
ALTERNATIVE SHIPPIN! PATTERNS FOR COASTAL SHIPPING
1.      At this time, two broadly different shipping patterns for
marine transport for small local ports are possible in Mozambique.
2.      The first (as originally set out in the Coastal Shipping Master
Plan) calls for integration of two independent shipping networks. A
regional network would transport cargo between the main ports using
vessels of 1500 deadweight tonnage (DWT) or more. At the same time a
local network will transport cargo between the priority small ports and
the closest main ports, using 500 DWT beachers. Transhipment would then
take place at the main ports to allow all the small ports to be
connected into the network. This shipping pattern has become known as
the '*asterplan' or the 'Beacher Plan".
3.      The second system envisages the transportation of cargo by
ships of over 1500 DWT by direct access between the small ports and all
the main ports. Since dredging the small ports to allow access for
these larger ships would be extremely costly, this system requires that
the larger ships anchor offshore and tranship cargo by smaller vessels
to the smaller ports. Two sub-systems have been proposed for this
operations
(i)   The first alternative is to use cargo ships of 1500 DWT
to 2500 DWT for the long haul shipping and to use
lighters, powered by port-bound tugs, to perform the
transhipments. This has become known as the "Improved
RAVIQUE Plan' or the "Lighter Plan".
(ii)  The second alternative is to use a custom-built barge
carrier of about 4000 DWT, which would carry about 40
barges along the coast, dropping empty barges and
collecting full ones at the small ports as it moves up
and down the coast. This has become known as the 'DELMAS
System" or the *barge carrier system".
Barge Carrieor          Feeder/Beach.r            Caroo/Liahters

4.      Given the poorer economics of transhipment in the regional
ports it is likely that most cargo bound for the bigger port. will go
via direct access, and local traffic wvil go via beacher. Likewise it
is unlikely that local traffic will be carried by the larger craft.
S.      The government's commitment to encouraging competition by
shipping lines in order to ensure efficient services, creates some
uncertainty as to the type of shipping pattern that vill emerge. It is
likely that more than one joint-venture shipping line wll be
established and wiil operate their shipping services based on their own
perception of what makes the most economic sense. It would be counter-
productive for the government to lay down the law to private shipping
companies. Hence, it is likely that multiple systems will operate.
6.      Since the optimal shipping patterns will in part be determined
by the level and mix of traffic, (which wiil in part be determined by
the security situation) it is difficult to predict the exact mix of
shipping patterns that will eventually become predominnt in these
areas.
7.      Further uncertainty is provided by the privatization of port
administration which may improve present port productivity
substantially. This may also affect the future shipping pattern.
S.      The exact structure of regional and mall coastal shipplng will
therefore be determined by market forces. Unfortunately planning for
port infrastructure requLres some understanding of likely future traffic
patterns. The main implicatLon of this li that any infrastructure
Investments to be made should aim for neutralLty between the two
system.
9.      Ship sizes and type of ship whlch are now being proposed are no
more than indicative and subject to further studles, dlscussion and
trLal by each shipping lines. According to available informatLon,
dimensions of vessels planned by shipplng llnes are as follows:

FIRST ROADS AID COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
Proposed Institutional Arranements for Small Ports
Overview
1.    The Government of Mozambique has agreed to transfer the operation of the
small ports from CFM to private *terminal cargo handling companies" in return for
a concession fee. Subject to meeting the service conditions of the concession
the operators will be granted the freedom to manage the ports in a commercial
manner. The first ports to be awarded to concessionaires will be Mocimboa da
Praia, Angoche, Pebane, Chinde and Macuse (the five small ports under this
project). At a later date concessions may also be extended to other small ports.
In the light of the importance of this regulatory change, a condition of the
utilization of the credit line for investments in each of the small ports will
be the execution of a concession agreement for that port.
2.    Responsibility for the small ports will first be transferred from CFM to
the DNH in the Ministry of Transport and Communications (MTC). These facilities
will then be awarded on a long term concession basis (ten to twenty years) in
return for a negotiated fee. The concessionaires will be able to choose whether
or not to take the existing equipment and will be allowed to choose which
employees they would like to continue to employ. (MTC would have to cover any
severance payments separately). The infrastructure investments envisaged under
this project will be decided upon by the concessionaire in conjunction with MTC
and will be financed from the project.  The concession fee will be suitable
adjusted for such investments to reflect the loan payments that the
concesssionaire would have had to have made, had the concessionaire taken out a
long term loan to cover the infrastructure costs.
3.    Training in port management and operations for the new concessionaires will
both be financed out of project funds, but the concessionaire will be expected
to cover at least part of the costs of these services.
Responsibilities and Regulations
4.    The concession agreement will clearly spell out the rights and obligations
of the company. The concessionaire will be free to: (i) set its own cargo
handling tariff; (ii) hire, fire and pay workers on a market related basis,
including the payment of incentive bonuses; and (iii) make investments as desired
(with the agreement of MTC).
5.    In return, the concessionaire will be required to: (i) handle third party
traffic in a non discriminatory way; (ii) clearly post and operate tariffs that
are non  discriminatory;  and  (iii)    perform  routine  maintenance  on  the
infrastructure.  The concessionaire will not be able to sell his concession
rights to another operator without the consent of MTC.
6.    The governemert will retain responsibility for the maintenance and marking



- 110 -
Annex 3-3
Page 2 of 3
of the channel (through INANINA).
7.    To be financially viable the franchise will need to be awarded over a long
enough period of time, however this will result in a monopoly for the successful
operator.   Furthermore the initial contract is likely to be awarded by
negotiation, not competition. While the operator should be allowed to charge
commercial rates, he cannot be allowed to ezercise his monopoly power through
extortionate rates. In negotiating the concession structure, provisions will be
made to mitigate this risk and to include some provision for transferring the
concession in the event of poor performance.   Significant regulation and
supervision of the port operator will therefore also be required.
8.    Although the concessionaire will have the right to make additional
investments if these are deemed justifiable, responsibility for the longer term
planning and major infrastructural investments will remain with the state. These
decisions will be made so as to be appropriate for the political environment as
it emerges in Mozambique. Bach port may have "supervisory committee" including
representatives of the Ministries of Transport, Trade and Agriculture as well as
the provincial governor. In the case of the larger ports local (city) government
may play a greater role. DNM will provide a 'technical secretariat' to perform/
undertake planning and supervisory functions, including the preparation of 7-10
year coastal shipping development plans.
Choosins the Concessionaires
9.    In a more developed competitive economy, companies may be short-listed to
compete for the concessions. However, in Mozambique, at this time, the market
is not yet inviting enough to attract competitive bids. It is therefore proposed
to enter into direct negotiations with potential port operators. In selecting
the port operators there are, in principle, three types of company that might be
interested - a shipper, a ship owner and an independent stevedoring company.
10.   Various shipping companies have already expressed an interest in operating
stevedoring services in some.  The following are target candidates among the
shippers for negotiating the concessions.  In most cases these are the main
shippers from each port and in many cases are already the de facto port
operators.
SWl I Port                                 Potentll Operator
Chind. and Zambexla River                  $ena Sugar
Anooeho                                    CaJu; Monica
Macuga                                     Sorror
Mo.loebt de Praia                          A'rico; Coupon. de Madeira; Manic.
11.   It might be possible to negotiate the concession with a joint venture
company comprising both a shipping company and a shipper or to negotiate
independently with two parties to obtain an element of competition.  To the
extent that it can be avoided shipping lines will not be allowed to operate as
10O concessionaires, but will be forced to operate as joint ventures.



- 111 -
Annex 3-S
Page 3 of 3
Fees
12.   The concession fee, in principle, will be related to the costs of the
infrastructure transferred to the concessionaire. In order to allow the operator
to be financially viable from the outset the fee will start at a lower level and
increase over time as traffic increases. This could be achieved through a flat
annual fee plus a fee related to the level of traffic. This will have the added
benefit of splitting the risk of the capital infrastructure investments between
the port operator and the government.
Viability of the Privately Operated Ports
13.   The small ports are currently not financially viable since the fees are
administratively set too low. However, in order for the concession scheme to
work the operation of the ports must be financially viable for the private
operators.   Preliminary analysis shows that it is possible to structure a
financially viable concession for the small ports. Howevoer the exact structure
of the concession fees will need to be negotiated between MTC and the operators.
Next Steps
14.  Action required, and tentative deadlines, to implement this strategy
includes

and to recommued changes to legislation
(iii) Ongoing technical assistance and training to help regulate the small
port concessions.

ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
TRAPFIC SCENARIOS FOR COASTAL SHIPPING IN MOZAMBIQUE
1.        The traffic forecasts are based on:
-       past commercialized production by major commodities in port
hinterlands;
-       previous maximum port throughputs by major commodities;
-       present commercialized production (including emergency aid);
-       assessment of production potential (year 2000) based ons
-    substantial improvement in security situation by  year
2000, which will imply a decrease in emergency aid;
-    assessment of present production by main    producers
and the family sector in the area;
-    indications of required investments in agricultural
processing units and rehabilitation of agricultural
areas, and source of financing;
-    growth rates by major crops and for some districts as
given in projections by the Ministry of Agriculture
2.        No detailed market studies have been carried out to ensure that
production will be competitive. Forecasts have been made based on interviews
w'th producers and port operators during field visits. A few controls of data
has been made through visits to the producers' headquarters in Maputo and
Quelimane.
3.        A summary of traffic in and out of each port is given in the table
attached to this annez. All ports increase their traffic over the analysis
period (1993-2012), although Pebane and Chinde should experience a decrease in
the first years due to reduced emergency aid. The growth rate differs between
ports and type of cargo in the interval 1993-2000, while a general increase of
41 annually is assumed for the interval .001-2012 for most types of cargo,
except for emergency aid which is assumed to disappear by the year 2000.
CRIND!
4.        Traffic through Chinde port itself has been and is very small and
mainly based on shipment of copra out and food aid in. Chinde has previously
been used as a transhipment point for sugar and molasses from the Sena sugar
factories at Marromeu and Luabo. However, transhipment of sugar took place
offshore, unloading barges from the factories to a floating warehouse" (old
vessel) anchored about 500 m offshore in the river estuary. Thus, the port of
Chinde will not be directly affected by possible revival of the sugar
production and exports, which might be in the range of 25 000 tons p.a. in
year 2000 (first stage of envisaged rehabilitation at Marromeu). Construction
materials for the rehabilitation of the sugar factories or inputs in future
production will not go through the port itself. The analysis does however
account for more consumer goods and general cargo coming into Chinde as a
result of any such development.
5.        About 10 000 tons of emergency aid (213 food and the balance, other
consumer goods) is said to arrive in Chinde this year. In addition, Copra



- 113 -
Annex 3 - 4
Page 2 of 5
from Sena (400 tons) and from the family sector (600 tons) will be shipped
this year to Quelimane and Beira.
6.        Forecasts for year 2000 assume substantial reductions in emergency
aid (from 10,000 to zero) and some increase in local copra production. No
figure on total productlon capacity in peace situation vas given, but it is
assumed that copra production can double. Including other goods in and out of
the port the traffic volume in 2000 is estimated to be about 8000 tons. Thus,
a reduction of volumes going through the port is envisaged by year 2000.
WACUSE
7.        Macuse is the transhipment point for copra from surrounding estates
and family production. Boror is the main producer and operator of the port. In
its peak year (1972) about 35 000 tons of copra went through the port (in and
out again), of which 18 000 tons came from Boror and the rest from the family
sector (and the other estates). 90 Z of the copra arrives by sea, 10 X by
truck. Boror picks up copra by barge from 4 central warehouses.
8.        Estimates of 1991 traffic range from 12-16 000 tons copra (into the
port and out again). Total tonnages handled in the Port are double i.e. 24 -
32 000 tons (since most copra arrives and departs by sea). In addition about 1
500 tons of food aid arrives by vessel this year. Other consumer goods come
from Quelimane by truck.
9.        Based on peace and some replanting of estates the production could
reach a maximum 25-30 000 tons by year 2000. Such an increase would require
infusion of new capital in Boror which most likely vill go into joint venture
vith a foreign company.
10.       Pebane is the transshipment point for copra from surrounding
estates, in particular Madal, Boror, C. de Zambezia and Morroa. Most of the
copra comes by barge to the port where it is unloaded to warehouses for later
shipment out.
11.       Mazimm quantities handled in the past (1981) vere 3 000 tons of
copra from Boror and 9 000 tons from the other estates. Apart from most of the
Boror copra which comes over land, these quantities are both into the port and
out again. In addition to copra about 5 000 tons of general cargo used to
come into the port. 1991 Boror production of copra was only 66 tons, while
that of other estates vas only at 9 000 tons. Total estimated food aid through
the port of Pebane in 1991 is 20 000 tons.
12.       According to Boror their maximum copra production could reach 5 000
tons vithin 5 years due to new plantings already made and further
rehabilitation/replanting of the estates. Production of the other estates is
assumed to stay as today. In addition to these volumes it estimated that about
2 000 tons of general cargo will go into the port. It is assumed that food aid
is stopped by year 2 000 due to improvements in security situation.



- 114 -
Annex S - 4
Page S of S
ANGOC
13.       Angoche is the outlet for an important caju (cashew) and groundnut
producing area in Mozambique. Wood and cotton is produced in its hinterland.
At present there are only two state-owned caju factories working, i.e. Caju I
and Caju II. The most recent maximum throughput of caju in the port was 15
000 tons in 1987. This production was halved in 1989 due to the security
situation. The production for 1990/91 was 7 500 tons, of which 4 000 tons went
by ship and the rest by road to Nacala (380 km away) due to lack of shipping
capacity. In addition, about 800 tons of wood went out of the port and nearly
6 000 tons of maize, oil, general cargo came in. Local transport of goods by
small vessels amounts to 800 tons, handled on the beach outside the port area.
14.       The forecast for year 2000 is based on the assumption that the 1987
caju production and throughput in the port can be reestablished, i.e. 15 000
tons out. Apparently the district of Angoche is comparatively rich and well-
run, and the major constraint for increased caju production seems to be the
security situation for the family sector. In addition it is assumed that the
present 7 000 tons of maize, salt, wood and general cargo will grow to 8 500
by year 2000.
OCIMB0A DZ PlATA
15.       Mocimboa de Praia is the main outlet for a rich wood and maize
producing area. In addition caju (1000 tons in 1990) and beans are shipped out
of the port. Agricom has stopped the purchase of maize from the family sector
because their warehouses are full and there is a lack of shipping capacity.
Some maize and beans are therefore trucked to Pemba (350 km). Total
commercialized production of maize in 1990 was 4 000 tons, of which 3 400 went
through the port, giving a total of agricultural produce of nearly 5 000 tons.
16.       Production of wood is also limited by lack of port/shipping
capacity. In 1990, 8 000 tons of wood was exported through the port, and 200
tons were trucked to Pemba. The acreage available for logging 125 000 ha, of
which 22 000 ha are worked at present. Immediate increase in log export given
better port/shipping conditions was said to be 60?.
17.       The port of Mocimboa appears to limit present production, and
benefits of port/shipping improvements can lead to increased production.
Projections of future traffic have taken this into account. Thus, about 20 000
tons of wood and 10 000 tons of maize and other agricultural produce are
projected to pass through the port in year 2000.

FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
NM ORGANIZATIONAL RESPONSIBILITIES FOR COASTAL SHIPPING
Oraanization of the Coastal Shipping Sector
Current Structure
1.    Responsibility for the management of the coastal shipping sector
and the development of the small ports currently lies with DNM. Under
DNM there are a number of parastatal companies responsible for the
operation of transport services and infrastructure. These include
Navique, Transmaritima, Emarna, Inahina and Escola Portuaria. Recently
the Bureau of Maritime Projects (GAPROMAR) was created to manage the co-
ordination of the different development projects within the coastal
shipping sector.
Future Structure
2.    The future vision for DNM sees the following shift in emphasis
(see diagram on next page).
a.    The parastatal operating entities will acquire more
managerial and commercial independence. Some companies will be
formed into companies with DNM controlling the Government's share.
Some companies may be fully divested. Others may stay under DNM's
control in the longer term (eg. Inahina). DNM's role will become
more supervisory than managerial, with management and
implementation responsibilities allocated to the individual units.
b.    DNM wIlll be responsible in the short to medium term for the
restructuritg of the small ports and the coastal shipping sectors
to allow greater competition and private sector participation,
concentrating on changing the regulatory environment and
negotiating small port and shipping concessions.
c.    In the medium to long term DNM will assume planning and
regulatory responsibilities for the small ports and shipping
sector, as well as safety and security in the ports. This
includes (i) monitoring the port and shipping concessions,
including the preparation of a status report on conditions in all
ports at least every two years, (ii) reviewing tariffs to ensure
that monopoly exploitation is avoided; (iii) ensuring that
development of the sector is co-ordinated with other economic
developments (iv) development of 7-10 year development plans for
coastal shipping and ports.
d.    GAPROMAR will remain as a "projects unit" within DMN
responsible for the co-ordination and supervision of ongoing
maritime development projects (eg. Quelimane, Pemba) and for donor
co-ordination. This will include monitoring the progress of
project implementation, compiling reports for donors and ensuring
that accounts are maintained and audited by external auditors. It
is not envisaged that GAPROHAR will get into the details of civil



- 118 -
Annex 3-5
Page 2 of 2
design work and feasibility studies, but will manage the civil
works consultants to be funded under the project.
e.    DNM will retain limited administrative functions for their
own office management and budgetary control.
f.    DNM will maintain a training unit, responsible for planning
the training of its own staff and for developing a training
program for the entire coastal shipping sector.
3.    Agreement on a vision for DNM and GAPROMAR will need to be
obtained at negotiations.

4.    In the short to medium term expatriate assistance will be needed
in most of these functions. However, DNH will concentrate on developing
the human resources required to perform the regulatory duties, planning
and supervision as a matter of priority. Engineering skills will be
bought in'. However, should DNH take on a broader supervisory role
with respect to the larger ports, it might be necessary to build up some
limited in-house supervisory engineering skills.
S.    In order to develop this capacity, technical assistance and
studies for DNH/GAPROMAR will includes
a.    Short term assistance and studies to restructure the
shipping and ports industries as in 2(b) above.
b.    Longer term assistance to perfonm the supervisory roles
until such time as this can be transferred to Mozambicans.
c.    An organizational study to clarify and build commitment to
the new DNM organizational structure.
d.    Short term assistance to strengthen DNM's management
capability.
e.    A longer term organizational study to clarify DNH's
relationship vis-a-vis the other units in MTC, especially with
respect to strategy and planning for the large ports.

MDLUMBIQVS
FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
ASSESSNENT OF TRANSPORT SERVICES PEORMANCE
1.    This review covers intermodal transport logistics.  It includes
freight-forwarding, customs brokerage and other related services
provided by such institutions as insurance companies, customs and banks
on imports/exports and transit of freight.
2.    At the outset it is important to note that existing data is
scarce, not always reliable and sometimes contradictory. There is an
overriding need for consistency in collecting and processing
information. With regard to transport services, this need is addressed
in the proposals for an action program in Annez 4-11.
I.    Rol, of Private Bnterprises and Private Sector Prospects.
3.    The key logistic chain services for international and domestic
traffic (including transfers to coastal shipping and transit traffic to
landlocked countries) is served by mainly parastatal companies, most of
which lack motivation, are bankrupt technically and do not offer the
service standards to ensure expeditive and cost effective services.
There are a few international joint venture private operators offering
mainly ship agency services, including limited coastal shipping, and a
very limited number of domestic private sector transport intermediaries
offering a variety of services including inland transport.
4.    An assessment of the services provided by the public sector
enterprises is not favorable. At the end of April 1991, there was a
backlog of over 700 import containers at the port of Maputo waiting for
clearance. At the same date, about 800 containers in transit to
domestic destinations were waiting for coastal shipping capacity. The
dwell time at port for over 602 of these containers exceeded 50 days.
5.    Of the 2.42 million tons of international freight handled in
Maputoi (excluding national freight, and oil-products which are handled
at MWtola port), 1.34 million tons (55Z) were transit freight to South
Africa, Zimbabwe and Swaziland. This high percentage of transit freight
is only indicative of the potential existing, which is now partly
channelled through other ports (mainly South Africa).
6.    Poor performance of the logistic chain in Mozambican ports in
terms of transit time results in cost increases and contributes to the
vulnerability of cargo which insurance coverage expires sixty days after
unloading from vessel. As a result, the Southern African and Eastern
Africa Conference (SAEC) Conference has imposed a surcharge of US$300
per TEU on Maputo-bound freight and may increase it to US$600 if there
is no improvement in delays, damage and pilferage. In the same manner,
poor risk performance by Mozambique has influenced an increase of
between 251 and 501 in re-insurance rates.



- 120 -
Annex 3-6
Page 2 of 8
7.    As a result, the costs for port handling and storage in transit on
imports account for a hefty 21Z of C & F costs in Mozambique. If
insurance and reinsurance premiums are added, costs in Mozambique ports
are a deterrent to transit traffic.
8.    On freight transfers between domestic ports by coastal shipping,
the present tariff rates are extremely high. At an average of US$40 per
ton, these rates for container cargo for coastal domestic transfers
compare poorly with international shipping rates in the sub-region
(US$50 Karachi-Maputo as an example) and add a new cost element to the
high price of imports. Opening of coastal shipping to a more
competitive environment should yield cost savings of between 30 and 352
which is the estimated premium now paid for the inefficient operation of
the parastatal fleet.
9.    An assessment of the operational characteristics, administration,
staff motivation, financial situation and performance of the five public
enterprises suggests that substantial gains in cost effectiveness and
efficiency could be obtained by encouraging competitive practices while
assisting the port of Haputo in privatization of port handling and
stevedoring operations, and the customs administration in modernization
and improvement of its operations. At stake are the competitiveness of
Mozambique regional freight transport corridors and the potential
financial fall out (foreign currency earnings) normally associated with
a sound and competitive freight forwarding industry.
10.  The following issues are important factors contributing to cost
and undesirable performances
(i)   poor coordination, control and handling of cargo at all
regional portst
(ii)  poor development of freight forwarding;
(iii) complexity of documents and procedures in the logistic chain
including transport, customs and trade documents;
(iv)  insufficient responsiveness of transport intermediaries to
loss and damage;
(v)   restrictive insurance practices;
(vi)  poor financial liquidity of importers; and
(vii) deficient communications.
11.  Ln addition to the above issues, the current security situation in
Mozambique affects sound modal interface between rail and road transport
by drastically curtailing railway services.



- 121 -
Annex 3-6
Page 3 of 8
II. Analysis of Issues
12.   Poor coordination, control and handling of cargo at Maputo Port
The traditional railway domination in Mozambican logistics has not yet
found a compromise with road transport. New stevedoring, port handling
or dispatching techniques adapted to container handling and to modern
requirements should be essential to produce productivity gains 1/.
Although the handling equipment maintenance condition is not good enough
to allow good rates of equipment availability (as an example, the
container cranes were delivered eight years ago without spare parts nor
operational training and have not had significant maintenance ever
since), motivation, training and management seem to be the most
overriding needs. Marginal gains in freight unitization could be
translated into substantial savings in time and therefore in cost (as an
example 'thruputs of unitized cargo at the 'liner traffic' pier is 900
to 1000 tons per 24 hours against 500 to 700 tons at the NAVIQUE's non-
unitized coastal shipping pier).
13.   Issues affecting international transfers at the port handling and
stevedoring level can be summarized as follows:
(i)   poor equipment availability as a result of scarce foreign
currency to procure spare parts and lack of maintenance
21!
(ii)  poor coordination of port logistics due to lack of
locomotive power for shunting operations and not enough
planning for alternative modal choices; as a result, slow
turn-around of railcars at port drastically curtails railcar
capacity availability;
(iii) substantial pilferage, in particular on food aid
consignments, and high rates of damage partly as a result of
long dwell-times, partly as a result of poor security;
(iv)  poor motivation resulting from low salary levels;
(v)   insufficient know-how of modern equipment capabilities,
means of efficient use, and equipment management in general,
at middle management level; and
(vi)  poorly designed port logistics for intermodal operation.
I/    Actual container Ithruput' is 12 TEU's per hour against a
potential of 20 TEU's per hour with similar ANSALDO type gantry
cranes in other ports.
21    Craness 29 operational out of 59.
Fork-lifts: 54 units standard specification, 3 units for timber, 3
units for containers with 50Z availability rate.



- 122 -
Annex 3-6
Page 4 of 8
14.   Two on-going studies should be instrumental in providing knowledge
of ways and means to improve port operations:
Mi)   The Port Master Plan (Technical Assistance Project)
completed on April 1991 by PORTIA with ODA financing.
(ii) Study on privatization of stevedoring operations (this
latter study does not seem to include privatization of port-
handling operations which would have been complementary).
15.   In addition, two on-going projects will attend to limited needs in
equipment rehabilitation:
(i)   HPC Project (AMN) financed with German assistance; and
iii) RELIABLE Project for the rehabilitation of CLARK fork-lifts,
financed with own-funds.
16.   Poor development of freight-forwarding:  A by-product of the
dominance of the public enterprises in international logistics is the
poor development of freight-forwarding capabilities. ANFRENA 3/
does not have the capabilities to compete with regional counterparts on
transit traffic nor the expertise and motivation to do an expeditive job
in parastatal-captive traffic. MANICA, a joint-venture of South-African
and regional interests handles substantial transit traffic, has an
efficient but small coastal shipping operation, but is limited to handle
private sector interests. MOCARGO, originally a freight bureau for the
control of freight rates on movements of parastatal cargo, has recently
started to compete successfully on transit and private sector movements
and shows management potential and capabilities. A smaller fourth
freight-forwarding operation competing on private sector business with
MANICA and MOCARGO completes the freight-forwarding scenario.
17.  Lack of competition in freight-forwarding contributes to high cost
of services. Comparatively, transit through Maputo to South Africa
,Swaziland, and to a lesser extent Zimbabwe, would be the natural
choice. Transit prices from FOB vessel Haputo to Mozambican border for
South-Africa and Swaziland are, however, about 62Z of the through
freight rate from Lisbon to inland borders

-     Sea freight Lisbon/Haputo             34           125
-     CAP/BAF and surcharge                  4            15
-     Port handling                         14            50

railcars.                             48          175
3/    The Agencia Nacional de Prete e Navigacao (ANFRENA) employs 536
persons and, in the first quarter of 1991, handled 145,000 tons of
stevedoring and 915 tons of transit.



- 123 -
Anne: 3-6
Page 5 of 8
18.   Poor development of freight-forwarding results in all freight
transactions negotiated on C + F terms on import and FOB terms on
exports, with all international parts of the trade contract the
responsibility of Hozambique's trade partner. These contracting
practices reduce the potential for foreign-currency earnings on
international transport contracts to Mozambican transport operators (in
particular international freight-forwarders). In today's competitive
and container dominated multinational transport scenario, freight-
forwarders act as multinational operators and are able to attract to the
countries from where they operate substantial financial flows (payables
and receivables) much in the same way airlines or shipping companies
do, but with less or marginal investment.
19.   Complex documents and procedures:  The Mozambican documentary
process has been partially modernized since independence.
Modernization, however, is limited to the most critical documents, such
as the 'customs declaration,. Other documents and procedures need to be
laid-out to the UN standards prior to introduction of electronic
coimunications. Facilitation of documents will reduce the odd forty
pieces of documentation needed for an import documentary declaration
(including banking, trade, customs and transport documents) to
three/four basic formats with a maximum of ten final documents.
20.   The complexity of procedures attached to the documentary process
is substantial with numerous signatures and seals at each stage of the
process. Once documents are facilitated, procedures may be reduc6d
coinciding with the introduction of electronic co_munications.
21. The compatibility of the customs, port, freight-forwarders and
banking computer programs will be essential and needs to be planned
ahead of time.
22.   Two projects aiming at the simplification of customs
administration procedures are currently under way (or awaiting
approval):
(i)   Project MOZ/89/018, financed by UNDP (US$238,000) and
executed by UNCTAD, provides for support to the customs
administration and has the following objectives:
*     improve collection of revenues on international trades
*     harmonize the legal basis, mechanisms and procedures;
and
*     study the feasibility of economic and technical
external assistance to improve the customs
administration.
(ii) Project aAF186/046 is a joint UNDP|UNCTAD regional project
executed by UNCTAD which has already produced some valuable
results such as the introduction of the coupon system for



- 124 -
Annez 3-6
Page 6 of 8
transit traffic on the Halawi/Hozambique/Zimbabwe corridor.
It has the three following objectives:
*     facilitation of transit traffic;
*     introduction of modern data management techniques
related to the introduction of electronic
communications; and
*     monitoring of cargo movements (ACIS);
23.   None of the two UNCTAD executed projects provides, however, for
the facilitation of documents and procedures. The facilitation of
customs, transport and trade documents seems an essential step forward
while administrative regulations and procedures related to transport and
trade are redrafted and simplified and while a decision on the
computerization of customs is made as part of project M02/89/018.
24.   UNDP would be willing to increase financing under project
MOZ/89/018 to include document facilitation. This item will be part of
the intermodal facilitation program proposed under the project.
25. Insufficient responsiveness of transport intermediaries to loss
and/or damages Mozambican ports and, in particular, Haputo port are
considered high risk by international reinsurance companies and a
surcharge of US$300 is levied by the SAEC Conference on Haputo-bound
freight. The liability provisions of transport operator (road, rail and
water) go back to pre-independence days and are not adapted to modern
comodity values and to container traffic. The same applies to terminal
operators (Maputo port) and freight-forwarders. Under present
provisions, recovery of damage/loss would be regulated by liability
principles no longer reflecting reality.
26.  Updating liability regulations under curreut commercial law, and
reviewing available transport liability conventions seems an essential
step to bring terms and conditions of carriage and terminal operators
responsibilities to modern standards.
27.   A review of current liability provisions followed by drafting of
modern terms and conditions of carriage for transport and terminal
operators will be proposed as part of the intermodal facilitation
program.
28.   Insurance practices:  A draft proposal that all imported freight
into Mozambique be subject to compulsory domestic insurance coverage, if
enforced, would uimediately result in higher insurance premiums for
Mozambican-bound cargo. At present only parastatal freight imported
into Mozambique is subject to compulsory coverage by EMOSE (Empresa
Mozambicana de Seguros). The recomendation follows UNCTAD's general
attitude towards protection of shipping and insurance of domestic
interests in developing countries. Recommendations concerning insurance



- 125 -
Annex 3-6
Page 7 of 8
were initially made by UNCTAD in the 1960's and have resulted, in a West
African example, in a proliferation of insurance monopolies not in the
best interest of the countries. Review of UNCTAD's rationale behind the
encouragement of domestic insurance is urgently needed.
29.   In the meantime, urgent action would be required in two areas: (a)
procedures for compliance by shippers and operators with provisions for
reporting damage upon arrival of cargo to Mozambican ports, and (b)
procedures for compliance by shippers and freight-forwarders with
provisions for extending risk coverage at port beyond the 60 days
coverage for imported goods in transit or awaiting for clearance.
30.   Poor financial liquidity of imDorters:  A high percentage of the
containers awaiting clearance at the Haputo port cannot have the
documentary credit provisions confirmed by the Bank of Mozambique due to
lack of counter value deposits in local funds by the importer (often a
parastatal company) or lack of funds for customs duties. As a result,
containers and general cargo must be left at port, usually beyond the
sixty days insurance coverage period, until documentary credits are
confirmed.
31.  Since the financial liquidity of importers is not likely to
improve in the near future, it is suggested to change current import
license fund deposit provisions to make compulsory deposit by importers
of lOOS of countervalue funds, or to support their import license
request with an adequate bank commitment accordingly. Otherwisp, the
situation of containers stored in the Maputo port will worsen.
32.   Deficient communications:  Cosmunications within Hozambique and
with the sub-region are poor. For customs and transport purposes
deficient communications are one of the most important problems facing
introduction of modern standards and practices. Computerization of
customs and transport documentation will be contingent on better
communications. Although not within the scope of this project,
assistance to communications is clearly a priority area.
III. Transport and Trade Stratemy
33.   Introducing competition and cost effectiveness in domestic and
intermodal transit logistics, and in freight-forwarding, would go a long
way towards improving the balance of payments account deficit by
increasing the non-factor services (transport and insurance)
contribution in foreign-currency earnings.
34.  While dismantling public monopolies in transit logistics is
eminently desirable in the long term, actions can be taken in the short
and medium term to increase cost-effectiveness in transit services.
Such actions aret
(i)   facilitation of trade and transport documents and procedures
to UN lay-out key standards;



- 126 -
Annex 3-6
Page 8 of 8
(ii) making transport intermediaries more responsive to loss
and/or damage by updating legal liability levels for
carriers and terminal operatorsg
(UiL) reviewing insurance legal provisions and practices; and
(lv)  facilitating customs transactLons by improving communLcation
and computerizing operations.
35.   The proposed trade and transport facliLtation program should be
identified and prepared under the ROCS-1 project, and implemented under
the subsequent ROCS-2 project.

Amnez 3-7
Page 1 of 2
REPUBLIC OF MIOZMBIQA
FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
PRINCIPAL ACTImVTIES OF NORAD IN THE COASTAL SHIPPING SUB-SECTOR
1.          Coastal transport has been one of the main beneficiaries
over the past decade of bilateral cooperation between Mozambique and
Norway. Similarly NORAD has been the single biggest supporter of the
coastal shipping sector in Mozambique. The program level for both 1991
and 1992 is in excess of US$ 10 million annually, but this is expected
to be lower in 1993.
2.          NORAD has been, and is continuing to, assist the MTCIDNM in
a variety of fields, ranging from infrastructure and equipment
improvement to technical assistance in port management, personnel
training, and sound business management. As can be seen from below this
support has focussed on the marine sector as a whole, including 'support
organizations, and not very much on the cabotage transport that is the
main focus of the ROCS project. In that sense the historic NORAD
contribution (and its continuation) complements the ROCS project.
3.          The focus of NORAD cooperation has been increasingly
directed towards achieving sound economic and technical shipping and
port operations within the state institutions in charge of policy making
and management, with an eye towards future privatization of some of
these operations. Other key objectives are: (i) improvement of maritime
safety, with the establishment of a Maritime Safety Authority planned
for 1992; (ii) the more efficient channeling of emergency humanitarian
aid to needy people, especially the more vulnerable groups composed of
women and children; and (iii) support for environmental projects.
4.          Investment costs have been and will be kept at a low level
with the aim of maintaining a more flexible program which can be readily
modified to fit changing conditions when peace is achieved. Norway has
agreed to consider the possibility of supporting commercial cooperation
in the shipping field, including the establishment of joint ventures.
Current candidates for joint ventures are the shipyard, the transport of
dry cargo and the transport of petroleum products.
5.          A review of the bilateral cooperation program was carried
out in May 1991, and a number of recommendations resulted which have
been considered in future planning for the sub-sector. External audits
of NAVIQUE, INAHINA and EKARNA are scheduled to be introduced in 1992.
6.          Principal areas of cooperation are summarized below:
-     Institutional suDport to MTC/DHH, particularly to improve
maritime planning, to supply equipment for the local
shipping fleet, shipyards and ports, and to continue
assisting with training efforts.



- 128 -
Annex 3-7
Page 2 of 2
Construction of the Escola Nautica was completed and
expatriate and local instructors assigned. Some 100
students have already been trained, with the concept of
tuition fees being paid by users of the school having been
introduced so that the school will eventually become self
supporting.
A National Port Training Institute was established in 1988,
and is expected to be fully operational by 1993. ILO is the
executing agency, providing advisors to the port
administrative agency, CFM. A national manpower development
plan for the ports industry has been developed, and accepted
by Mozambican authorities.
The government institute, INAHINA, has been strengthened
through technical assistance in the hydrographic and
cartographic fields, including the training of personnel and
the acquisition of equipment. Two hydrographic teams were
trained and major parts of the Maputo entrance and fairway
vere surveyed and charted. This effort will be continued.
Assistance to NAVIQUE is continuing, with emphasis on
improved administrative practices, improved cargo handling
and ship maintenance, and personnel training. Steps will be
taken to help transform NAVIQUE into a commercially viable
public company through establishment of a joint venture, and
the present management contract will be extended through
1992. Creation of a tanker joint venture also is planned.
The state owned company, EMARNA, is being provided technical
assistance to improve its operation and financial position.
Principal technical objectives are to increase efficiency,
quality and general productivity of the shipyard.
Assistance was provided in the establishment of a coastal
shipping terminal at the Maputo port, with the passenger
terminal being completed in 1991. Rehabilitation of the
South Pier also was carried out with NORAD assistance.
Extensive rehabilitation/improvement works have been carried
out at the port of Pemba, and are expected to continue
through 1993.
Technical assistance in the engineering field was provided
to CFM, with close cooperation from local counterparts.
Main activities have been the coordination of project
preparation, including tender documents; the procurement of
materials and equipment; works supervision; financial
management of works projects; operational assistance in the
Pemba port; and personnel training.

MOZANIIQ_U
FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
DRAPT TEMS OF REERNCE FOR GENERAL CONSULTING SERVICES
TO THE NATIONAL DIRECTORATE OF ROADS AND BRIDGES (DNEP)
1   DESCRIPTION AND OBJECTIVES OF REQUIRED SERVICES
1.1       The Government of Mozambique's medium-term national road rehabilitation
and deferred-maintenance program, priority sections of which are expected to be
funded under the ROCS-2 project, is intended to bring most of the national
network back to pre-independence standards of serviceability and maintainability
by the end of this decade.   Because the DNEP staff is very limited, the
successful execution of this program so vital to the recovery of the nation's
economy requires the assignment of an international consulting firm to provide
highly experienced specialists for a broad range of technical assistance to DNEP
during at least the initial stage of the project (the services vill be required
Initially for three years, which is the term of the contract for which these
terms of reference (TOR) are written. This firm will act as General Consultant
(GC) to DNEP in all matters relating to its programs for roads and bridges, but
particularly with regard to implementation of the ROCS-2 project.
1.2       In addition to ensuring the timely and satisfactory implementation of
the roads component of the ROCS-2 project, a major objective of the consultancy
is the strengthening of the institutional capacity of DNEP.  These terms of
reference (TOR) describe the nature and scope of the services to be provided by
this General Consultant.
2. SCOPE OF WORK
2.1       General
2.1.1     The General Consultant to DNEP will have the responsibility for
coordinating and supervising, for and on behalf of DNEP, all consultants
providing technical services to the agency, monitoring their activities and
performance. In addition, it will provide advice and assistance directly to DNEP
in highway planning, organization, special studies and improved operational
procedures. Specific tasks to be performed are detailed in Section 2.2.
2.1.2     The services shall be carried out in accordance with generally accepted
standards of professional practice, following recognized engineering and
management principles and practices, with emphasis on effective use of available
resources. The scope of work of the CC includes all activities necessary to
accomplish the stated objectives of these services, while adhering to the
aforementioned principles and practices, whether or not a specific activity is
cited in the TOR.
2.1.3     The Team Leader of the GC will work closely with the National Director
of DNEP, and with the Project Coordinator (PC) for the ROCS project, keeping them
fully informed of the status of activities which the GC is responsible for
coordinating, monitoring or executing. He will advise and assist the Director



- 134 -
Annex 4-2
Page 2 of 7
as required to achieve the objectives of this technical assistance, particularly
regarding improvements in organizational effectiveness.
2.2       Principal Duties and Responsibilities
The General Consultant primarily will be required to carry out the
following tasks
*       Assisting the National Director of DNEP in implementing an
effective functional organization for general management of
highways and bridges included in the national road network;
*       Developing   and   implementing   a   simplified  management
information system (in consultation with the ROCS Project
Coordinator) for collecting and reporting data on projects and
technical assistance which the GC is responsible ror
supervising;
*       Recommending an updated functional classification of the
designated, non-urban, public roads of Mozambique;
*       Evaluating the present road maintenance management system and
assisting the DNEP Maintenance Department in extending that
system--with such modifications as are agreed as being
appropriate--to the entire national road network (to the
extent it is accessible);
3       Organizing and supervising the carrying out, by DNEP/DEP
personnel, of a detailed inventory and condition assessment of
the entire national road network (including drainage and other
structures), to the extent security conditions permit;
*       Performing a techno-economic study, at pre-feasibility level
using the HDM-I1 model (PC version), of the national road
network, and formulate a ten-year master plan for road and
bridge rehabilitation and improvement.
*       Evaluating,  with  the  assistance  of  the  HDM-III  model,
alternative strategies for periodic maintenance of paved and
gravel roads, and formulating a ten-year program of periodic
maintenance for roads improved under the ROCS-2 project, as
well as for other priority, primary and secondary roads.
*       Conducting a study of axle loads of heavy vehicles using the
national road network, and recommending a comprehensive
vehicle  weight  control  program,  including  fines  for
overloading. The possible use of tolls for heavy vehicles in
certain international corridors shall also be investigated;
*       Developing and costing a signing program for the national road
network, based on results of the nationwide road inventory
(taking into account that road rehabilitation projects
financed under ROCS-2 will include new signing);



- 135 -
Annex 4-2
Page 3 of 7
*       Conducting  a  brief  study  to  update  the  consultant's
conclusions and recommendations presented in 1987 regarding
the possible cost effectiveness of purchasing 3 plants to
produce bituminous emulsions, and 10 asphaltic pre-mix plants
to be used in road maintenance (possible feasibility of having
emulsions produced by private companies must be evaluated);
*       Formulating a  long term bridge  replacement program,  to
construct permanent bridges to replace many temporary
structures or where bridges are lacking, again drawing on
results of the nationwide road and bridge inventory;
*       Recommending measures which GOM might take to promote the
formation of small/medium local contracting firms, including
privatization of some existing ECHEPs;
*       Be responsible for monitoring the financial management of the
project, including proper operation of the project special
account which will be established as a revolving fund against
which authorized disbursements for project expenditures for
the roads component will be made.   The GC also will be
responsible for arranging an annual audit, within three months
of annual closing of accounts, by an independent firm of
auditors acceptable to IDA, of the deposits and withdrawals
from  the  roads  special  account--and  of  any  direct
disbursem nts to suppliers of goods and services--and for
ensuring that copies of the annual audit reports are forwarded
to IDA within six months of annual closing of accounts.
*       Providing such other technical assistance, mutually agreed by
DNEP and the GC, as may reasonably be required to fulfill the
objectives of these services, within the agreed contract
terms
2.3       Reports
2.3.1     The Consultant shall present to the National Director of DNEP, and to
the PC, within three months of the date of the Notice to Proceed with the
services required under these TOR, 10 copies of an Inception Report containing
a description of accomplishments to that date, and any modification to its
originally proposed work plan.   The report also shall include a detailed
description and flow diagram of the MTS to be used in monitoring and reporting
on all activities for which the GC is responsible. Two copies of the report must
be forwarded promptly to IDA. All reports to be produced in English and in
Portuguose.
2.3.2     Quarterly progress reports will be presented, in 10 copies, beginning
three months after the presentation of the Inception Report, summarizing the
activities during the reporting period under each major component of the
services, and the degree of compliance of other consultants with the respective
objective performance indicators. Actual versus planned expenditures by major



- 136 -
Annex 4-2
Page 4 of 7
project element also vill be indicated, as vill the principal activities and
costs of the GC itself. Any other information deemed necessary to adequately
inform the Director of DNBP, and the Project Coordinator--such as compliance with
legal covenants and credit agreements--will be included as well. Two copies of
these reports shall be furnished promptly to IDA.
2.3.3     Brief special reports will be presented if deemed necessary to
highlight problems requiring prompt action by DNEP3 such reports will contain the
Consultant's recommendations as to required action.
2.3.4     A Final Report on the consulting services rendered by the Consultant
will be presented upon completion of the services. Said report shall include a
pro-forma statement of the financial aspects of the assignment.
2.3.5     All documents and reports developed by the Consultant and paid for
under the study shall become the property of GOM upon completion of the
Consultant's services. Language of the documents and reports shall be English,
although a Portuguese translation of the Executive Summary of the Inception
Report and all regular progress reports will be provided by the Consultant.
Formats for the various reports will be agreed with DNEP and IDA.
2.4       Contract Period
2.4.1     The contract period for the services specified in these TOR shall be
36 months, unless otherwise agreed in the respective contract (as said contract
may be modified by mutual agreement). Services under the contract shall begin
within 30 days of receipt of the official Notice to Proceed. DNEP has the option
of canceling this contract for the convenience of the Government, as well as to
negotiate an extension if it so chooses.
2.5       Manpower Requirements
2.5.1     Services under the contract would be rendered by a team of resident and
short-term specialists, led by a senior highway engineer/planner with broad
experience in developing countries. Estimated requirements for provision of the
required services are:
*       Senior Highway Engineer/ (Team Leader) for 36 months.  Will be
responsible for coordination and direction of the consulting
team, and will work closely with the National Director of
DNEP, especially in matters of organization, policy, and
highway program management. Must be a graduate engineer with
at least 20 years of broad experience in the highway field,
with at least S years as manager of a technical assistance
team in a developing country (experience in Africa will be
advantageous).
*       Highway Planning and Design Engineer for 30 months. Will be
responsible for working with the DNEP road planning and design
staff, with particular emphasis on road classification and
inventory, and on establishing a continuing national road
planning capability using the HDK-III model or similar
evaluation tool. Also will assist DNEP in supervising the



- 137 -
Annex 4-2
Page 5 of 7
work of other consultants contracted to perform design
studies, and will train DREP counterpart staff in modern road
design techniques. Must be a graduate engineer with at least
15 years experience in highway planning and design, of which
at least 5 years must have been in developing countries, and
must have applied the 3DM-Ill model in highway planning.
*       Road Maintenance Engineer for a period of 18 months. Will be
responsible for assisting the DNEP Chief Maintenance Engineer
in further refinement of the existing road maintenance
management system (MMS) and extending it to the entire
national road network (as security and resources permit).
Must be a graduate engineer with at least 15 years experience
in the highway field, of which at least 5 must have been in
lmplementation of an MOS in developing countries, and must be
familiar with application of computerization to the MMS.
*       Bridge Engineer for 21 months.   Will be responsible for
vorking with his assigned DNRP counterpart to carry out a
detailed inventory, condition assessment and load rating of
all bridges on the national road network (to the extent
security permits); also will guide the development of a
national bridge program and will train his counterpart in
modern bridge design and construction techniques. Must be a
graduate engineer with at least 10 years experience in bridge
design and construction, and must have worked at least three
years in developing countries.
e       Traffic Engineer for 18 months.  Will be responsible for
working with his assigned counterpart in evaluating existing
traffic- investigation procedures and data, in planning and
re-establishing a nationwide program of traffic studies,
including both permanent and spot counting stations, and in
setting up a computerized traffic data base.  Must be a
graduate engineer with specialized studies, and at least 10
years experience, in traffic engineering, of which at least
two years must have been spent in developing countries.
*       Cost Accountant/Financial Analyst for 30 months.  Will have
responsibility for setting up and managing the special
accounts which will be established for any projects funded in
part by the World Bank or IDA, and in approving all
dlsbursements from said accounts; will work with DNEP
counterpart staff to ensure that adequate cost accounting,
reporting and auditing are performed for all DNEP programs,
including payments for contracted works. Must be a Chartered
Accountant (or CPA) with at least 15 years of experience, of
which at least 5 must have been in developing countries in
positions of similar responsibility.
*       Systems  Analyst for 27 months.   Will be responsible for

team members to develop and install, to the extent feasible,

paid the Consultant. Upon completion of these services all vehicles so procured
will become the property of GOM.

MOZAMIQlOU
FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT (ROCS-1)
ROAD TRAINING COMPONENT
I. Obiectives
1.1   Because of the acute shortage of qualified and experienced road
personnel, the main objective of the training component is to establish
DNEP's training capacity to enable it to start the first phase of a
long-term manpower development effort (15 to 20 years) aimed at
producing skilled, semi-skilled and managerial staff needed to
rehabilitate and maintain the national road network.
II.  Institutional Capaci
2.1   The objective mentioned above will be achieved in part through
revised training policies, more accurate staff planning and training but
mostly through institutional capacity building to be initiated under the
project and aimed at improving DNEP and Local Road Contractors' (LRC)
organizational, managerial, administrative, and legal requirements, as
well as long-term institutional development objectives needed for more
effective plann;ing and implementation -.! road activities. In addition,
the use of LRC to carry out road rehabilitation and maintenance works
is expected to increase thereby creating an atmosphere more conducive to
restore personnel motivation through better career development
opportunities. The shortage of personnel may ease some in late 1990s,
provided that salaries and other benefits become more competitive with
the private sector whose own demand for skilled staff will grow as its
activities expand.
III. Staffing and Training Capacity
3.1 Since independence the road subsector has been faced with serious
shortages of qualified and experienced staff at all managerial and
technical levels. Currently, 80Z to 90S of the established executive
and technical posts are either vacant or filled by staff lacking the
necessary qualifications. DNEP lacks experienced staff to plan and
monitor the work being done by LRC whose technical personnel also need
to be increased and upgraded. However, future recruitment of staff with
no experience in road vo;ks will not enhance road operations unless it
is supported by a comprehensive training program. Officially, DNEP is
responsible at national level for the planning and implementation of
training activities and, since the maid-1980s' has organized some
training courses but not as part of an agreed long-term training plan.
The overall results have been limited due to a variety of problems
includingS (i) weak institutional capacity; (ii) lack of sound training
policies and a long-term national training program; (iii) low level of



- 141 -
ANNEX 4 - 3
Page 2 of 8
education and literacy of most trainees; (iv) lack of funds; (v)
shortages of trainees because of lack of incentives and, most
importantly; (vi) government's weak administrative management system
which does not provide the right kind of motivation through competitive
salaries and career development opportunities.
3.2 With assistance from ILO, UNDP, SIDA and IDA, DNEP is taking steps
to establish its own training capacity to meet the road subsector
training needs at national level. Accordingly, it has issued a sound
training policy detailing action to be taken to deal more effectively
with staff training activities including career development linkages to
training and a system of professional standards and certification.
3.3    With the forthcoming introduction of policy, organizational and
administrative changes in road operations, training becomes a critical
element in transferring to the staff sufficient skills to perform at an
acceptable level. Existing and future road personnel will receive
training varying from one month to four years. Training courses would
be repeated as often as necessary, thus a high percentage of the
workforce would be trained and retrained more than once in the next 15
to 20 years depending on their ability to learn and retain. The
program is expected to involve a net total of about 3,500 personnel -
DNEP 100 and LRC 3,400 in main areas of activities detailed in
Attachment 2 of this Annex. However, since many workers would attend
more than one training course, the cuvmulative total of personnel to be
trained in the long-run would be considerably higher than 3,500,
depending also on transfer of workforce to the private sector, and
turnover (15-20Z).
IV. gleeuts of the Road Training Program
4.1 The training program will involve different categories of
personnel, theoretical training courses, and practical production
activities. The main elements of the program are: (a) training
technical assistance; (b) instructors; (c) the university (UEH),
selected technical institutes, specialized vocational schools, and
training centers; (d) training equipment and materials; (e)
accommucdation (housing); (f) transportation (for trainees and trainers);
(g) training abroad (fellowships); (h) training of counterparts; (i)
training allowances; and (j) project supervision and evaluation.
4.2 Training courses financed under ROCS-l will be available to all
interested persons/entities directly connected with the road subsector
on a recurrent cost recovery basis.
4 3   Support to MMZP and LIC Manoower Develoenet
a.    Trainia Devart nt. As part of the institutional support,
a Training Department will be established within DNEP to be
responsible for the continuous and systematic planning,
programing, implementation, supervision and evaluation of



- 142 -
ANNEX 4 - 3
Page 3 of 8
all DNEP's and LRC training activities. For the first
years, the Department will be staffed almost exclusively by
TA specialists. A senior manpower development specialist
will act as Chief Training Officer and head of the
Department, assisted by four training speciallsts, one of
them (financed by UNDP) dealing exclusively with the PRP.
The Chief Training Officer will report to the Director of
DNEP. The Department's main responsibilities will includet
(i) completing and continuing updating the DNEP and LRC
manpower inventory; (ii) assisting in establishing an
informal Training Steering Committee (TSC) within the
recently adopted training policy; (iii) formulating and
implementing the agreed five-year training program; (iv)
assisting in setting up the Road Technical Training Center
(RTTC), and the Road and Hechanical Training Production
Units; (v) coordinating training activities carried out by
TA specialists assigned to other project components; and
(vi) coordinating and supervising training activities
carried out by university and technical/vocational
institutes/centers. In addition, the Training Department
will ensure DNEP's senior staff and LRC directors
collaboration by making available experienced road and
mechanical supervisors to be trained as future instructors
(thereby replacing expatriate staff) and by releasing
existing and future personnel, in a timely way, to attend
agreed training courses and activities.
(b)   Tralnim Center (RTTC). Because of the considerable amount
of training expected to be carried out by DNEP in the next
15 to 20 years, ROCS-1 will provide funds to rehabilitate an
existing MCA's center to be renamed Road Technical Training
Center (RTTC). The RTTC is located in Chimoio (Manica
province) and will become the principal area where existing
and future DNEP and LRC supervisors, foremen, and
technicians would receive theoretical training and
upgrading. The Center currently includes two dormitories
for some 100 trainees, four classrooms, 2 workshops and
other facilities. A senior road training specialist will
act as Director and will be assisted by one administrative
officer and two road specialists with wide experience in
road rehabilitation and maintenance works, and management of
local road contractors. The RTTC will also be used to
provide training courses for administrative personnel, and
short seminars and workshops for mid- and high-level
managers. The Director of the Center will report to the
Read of the Training Department.
(c) load and Mechanical TrainAng Production Units (RTPU and
MM).  The purpose of these Units is to provide theoretical
(30S) and practical production training (70Z) to various
levels of DNEP and LRC road and mechanical personnel. The
RTPU would be the equivalent of a typical contractor



- 143 -
ANNEX 4 - 3
Page 4 of 8
maintenance unit, except that it will concentrate more on
training than production. Similarly, the MTPU would be the
equivalent of a small mobile workshop. Mechanical personnel
would be trained by repairing light and heavy road
equipment. The RTPU vill be headed by a senior expatriate
road foreman assisted by seven local supervisors/instructors
(roads, equipment operators, truck drivers and one
mechanic). The HTPU will be headed by a senior expatriate
mechanical foreman assisted by three local experienced
mechanics/instructors; and
(d)   Technical Assistance.  The project will provide Training TA
to implement training activities indicated in para (a), (b),
and (c) above totalling 568 man-months as followss

4.4   Trainin Nethodology
a.    All actions related to the preparation (Phase I) and
implementation (Phase II) of the five-year training program will be
planned and supervised by the Training Department in close collaboration
with the RTTC and the RIHTPUs. The type of training and the content of
courses will be defined through analysis of the particular functions and
occupatLonal job categories wLthin DNEP and LRC as indlcated in the plan
of action to be prepared by the Training Department. They will, include:
(i)  Seminar Workshops and other management courses for (i) LRC
mid- and high-level managers to improve the operational and
administrative quality of their activLtLes; and (ii) DNEP
senlor staff to help them carry out their increased
supervisory's responsibilities.
'ii)  Training and Upgrading Courses for exLsting instructors or
experLenced road and mechanical supervisors with potential
of becoming Lnstructors. They will require pedagogical as
well as technical training.
CiLL) Courses for Administrative Personnel such as procurement
officers, storekeepers, administrative assistants, clerical
staff and secretarial help.
Uiv) Training of Skilled and Semi-skilled Road Personnel such as
technicians, foremen, supervisors, equipment operators,
drlvers to be carried out mostly in the field (70Z) by
adopting a system of training through production (RTPU).
Initially one RTPU will be set up to conduct training while
carrying out rehabLlitation and maintenance activLties on
specific asphalt, gravel, and feeder roads sections wlthin
DNEP's annual maintenance progrm. Personnel assigned to
the RTPU would be trained, therefore, under actual working
conditions. In addition, most personnel would also receive
theoretLcal (audio-visual) training (302) at the RTTC.
(v)   Training of Mechanical Personnel to be carried out mostly by
the Mechanical Training Production Unit (MTPU). In addition
to some general educational and theoretical courses, the
trainlng of mechanics would focus on the repair of broken
equipment under supervision of ezperienced mechanical
instructors, thus enhancing LRC equipment maintenance
capabLlity and raLsing their level of equipment
availability.
(vl) Training of Feeder Roads Workers to be carrLed on on-the-job
by experienced supervisors during the labor intensive
brigades normal work activLties.

ANNEX 4 - 3
Page 7 of 8
(vii) Training Abroad (Fellowships). Short- and medium-term
fellowships will be granted, after positive experience with
project and training TA staff, to counterparts such as
managers, senior staff, personnel to be assigned to the
Training Department, RTTC, and R/MTPUs. Fellowships would
be properly planned and supervised with participants
agreeing tot (i) visit countries whose language they are
fluent in; (Wi) serve DNEP and LRC for a period of five
years; and (iii) write reports applicable to their work
prior to their return home.
V. Training ZouApment and Materials
5.1   As no training equipment is currently available, the project
provides funds for this element of the program as followst
(a) Training equipment and materials for RTTC includings (i)
audio-visual training aids and facilitie to prepare
coursest (ii) courses obtained from ILO and IRF, or from
Portugal, Brazil, etc.; (iii) computer hardware and
software; (iv) photocopy machines; and (v) others.
(b)   Equipmentimachines and materials required by the Training
Department.
(c) Heavy road rehabilitation/maintenance equipment, as well as
fuel, materials, cement, etc., for the RTPU; and 1 mobile
workshop, hand tools, workshop equipment, and spare parts
for the HTPU.
VI. Training Allowance
6.1   A training allowance will be provided under the project since
usually local staff of the Training Department, instructors and trainees
are not sufficiently motivated to participate in extensive training
courses (loss of income from outside work activities and additional
living expenses while attending courses outside their home base). In
addition to this allowance, instructors' regular salaries should be in
line with those of high level technicians.
VII. Prolect Evaluation
7.1   The project provides financing for an independent evaluation of
the training program to be conducted at the end of the second year (mid-
1994) and near the end of the program (mid-1997), including
recommendations for further training activities (ROCS-2).



- 147 -
ANNEX 4 - 3
Page 8 of 8
VIII. Supnort to the University of Eduardo Mondlane (MEN). Technical
Education and Vocational Training
8.1 Fifteen years after independence, notwithstanding some substantial
progress, especially in the development of a higher education system,
basic human resources development remains as the country's single
greatest need. A number of studies are currently underway aimed at
diagnosing the education sector's problems, and recommending appropriate
steps to improve it. The Bank has assisted UEM in the past through the
First and Second Education projects which have provided funds for the
Faculty of Engineering to enable it to switch to more practical training
through the equipping of basic laboratories in applied thermodynamics,
electrical engineering, and applied electronics. Materials for the
mechanical workshop, and computer hardware and software were also
included. ROCS-1 provides funds for the Department of Civil Engineering
(DCE) to finance (i) the salary and travel of four professors recruited
from Chile; (ii) the construction of four houses for the new professors;
(iii) two computers with software; and (iv) technical books. It is
expected that during the period 1993-4/1998 a total of 20 DNEP new staff
will enroll at the University to obtain a five-year civil engineering
degree.
8.2 Technical and vocational education will be strengthened and
expanded with funds provided under the project (SIDA and IDA , including
the reestablishment of the three-year diploma course for road and bridge
technicians at the Industrial Institutes of Maputo and Beira.
8.3 ENTREPOSTO, a well known automotive private company in Mozambique
which conducts various training courses, will assist DNEP with training
of LRC mechanical instructors and senior mechanics. The project will
provide funds to finance agreed training courses.
8.4 Centro de Formacao Industrial (CFI), established in 1987 with
financial and technical assistance provided by SIDA, UNIDO, and other
entities, conducts a variety of training courses and seminars in
business management and related administrative services. Funds are
included in the project to enable DNEP and LRC administrative personnel
to attend these courses/seminars expected to be conducted by CFI at the
RTTC.
IX. Cost Estlmtes
9.1 The total cost of the training program is estimated at about
US$28.3 million equivalent as detailed in attachments to this Annex.
