MOZAHIQlU
FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SEIPPING PROJECT
Road Transport Operations - Proposed Action Prograr
1.      The ROCS project'vill include studies/policy initiatives in
support of improvements in road transport operations. The proposed
studies should document the rationale for the proposed policy
initiatives, make proposals for improved use of resources and
dissemination of maintenance capacity, and propose financial instruments
for implementation of the studies recommendations.
2.      The proposed studies should be Crouped into a program, 'the
road transport privatization and rehabilitation program", and should be
followed up by the project coordinator until completion. Once
completed, implementation and follow-up of the studies' recommendations
could be entrusted to an "ad-hoc' committee. Proposals for this
committee are made in this Action Program.
3.      The proposed studies are designed to develop:
(a)   guidelines for shifting the balance of the trucking
industry from the public to the private sector. The
proposed guidelines will assess the need for funding to
effectuate privatization of the parastatal fleets and of
appropriate procedures and financiLl intermediaries -
based on recognized commercial and banking practices - to
move such funds. In the above context, the guidelines
will examine the potential role of cooperative
associations in facilitating funding and vehicle
transfers and in providing economies of scale for spare
parts procurement;
(b)   guidelines for the liberalization of regulations and
other restrictions on private sector road transport
activities. These will substantiate the need for support
services to develop cost-based tariffs and regulatory
guidelines suitable to encourage transport activities to
mnimum. Revision of existing minimum standard for
insurance, security and safety in the road transport
industry, and proposals for cost-effective means to
advocate these standards, will be part of the guidelines.
(c)   identification of types of training and/or assistance
needed by emnorging private sector operators and
identification if training and/or assistance needed by
GOM agencies to 'ulfill the reduced and re-oriented GOM
role in road transport activities; and
(d)  assessment of the potential for rehabilitation of
existing vehicle resources; in particular assessment of
the mechanical condition of parastatal fleets, including



- 158 -
Annex 4-6
Page 2 of 3
economic criteria for rehabilitation, requirements for
mechanical components and vehicle replacement needs.
This would be complemented by an assessment of mechanical
skills at the workshop floor level, and would be
complemented bys (i) proposals for deregulation of
workshop licensing related to installation of new
capacity; (ii) review of the adequacy of regulations
related to distribution of imported spare parts and
components, and (iii) facilitation of foreign exchange
allocation for workshop equipment, spare parts and
components.
Format of the Proposed Program
4.      The proposed program will have the format of pre-investment
studies containing actions and proposals suitable for imediate
implementation, either through the USAID private sector development
program or, alternatively, through the proposed Roads Project of IDA,
and should link the Coastal Shipping Project with subsequent IDA
investment operations.
5.      The studies proposed under the program will be divided into two
separate partst
(a)  an initial study effort aimed at providing primary
information on the prospects and most suitable channels
of privatization (including financial intermediary
channels) on the one hand, and on the most critical areas
of trucking regulation in need of liberalization, on the
other. The initial study effort would be financed by an
USAID Program Development Support facility which could be
available in early 1992. The initial study would be
completed at the latest by June 1992; and
(b)   the main study effort which could start as soon as
procurem nt of contractual arrangements of the study
consultants is completed by USAID. The estimated timing
for the start of the main study is in fourth quarter of
1992.
6.      Both efforts will be supervised jointly by GOH and USAID, with
support from IDA.
7.      The road transport privatization and rehabilitation program
should be anitored and coordinated by a local committee which would
liaise with the ROCS project coordinator office, with the 'ad-hoc
institutions and with the Bank. This commlttee could be integrated for
all practical purposes as a sub-sectoral group of the Transport and
Trade Facilitation Committee.

MOZAMBIQUE
FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
COASTAL SHIPPING TRAINING COMPONENT
I. Ob1ectives
1.1        Because of the shortage of qualified and experienced port
personnel, the main objective of the training component is to improve the
skills of selected coastal ports and shipping companies' personnel to enable
them to carry out their activities more efficiently and thus improving
maritime operations.
II. Institutional Capacity
2.1        The objective mentioned above will be achieved in part through
revised training policies, technical assistance and well structured training
activities, but most4y through institutional capacity-building. Steps are
being taken by GOH/MTC to adopt and promulgate a firm policy commitment to
promote and maintain full competitiveness in the coastal shipping transport
sub-sector, and to transfer ownership and responsibility for the small ports
from CFM.EE to DNKjGAPROMAR with appropriate provisions to grant long-term
contracts to experienced concessionaires. It is expected that policy reforms
and substantial privatization of the sub-sector would restore personnel
motivation through competitive salaries and career development opportunities.
III. Staffing and Training Capacity
3.1        Currently, the estimated full-time workforce of the five ports
being assisted under the project - Angoche, Macuse, Mocimboa de Praia and
Pebane - totals 227 (an average of 45 per port) broken down as follows: 117
skilled and semi-skilled personnel, 40 clerks, 10 port managers and deputy
managers and 60 seamen/captains (tug crews). A total of 800 casual labor is
employed by the ports as needed. As most of the skilled and semiskilled
personnel never received formal training, their qualifications and experience
are inadequate. This problem is further compounded by-the country's low
levels of education and literacy and low salary scales. DNH/GAPROHAR are not
adequately staffed and lack the necessary organizational structure to manage
the coastal shipping subsector which also requires more qualified and
experienced personnel. However, recruitment of personnel without previous
experience in port activities will not improve coastal shipping operations
unless they are properly trained ( except in those cases where concessionaires
provide their own management and supervisory personnel).
3.2        Officially, the Training Department of MTC is responsible for the
planning and implementation of training activities but, so far, overall
results have not been satisfactory due to (i) weak institutional capacity;
(ii) lack of fundes and (iii) shortages of trainees because of lack of
incentives and other constraints. Whether the coastal shipping companies and
small ports are managed by DNH/GAPROMAR or contracted to main concessionaires,
training of personnel at all levels is required in either case. With



- 164 -
Annex 4-8
Page 2 of 3
assistance from donors and IDA, DNK/GAPROMAR are taking steps to assist with
the subsector's training needs. Accordingly, training guidelines have been
issued detailing action to be taken to ensure staff development activities
including career development linkages to training and a system of professional
standards and certification.
3.3        With the forthcoming introduction of policy and organizational
changes in maritime operations, training becomes a critical element in
transferring to the staff technical skills to perform at a satisfactory level.
Ezisting and future personnel will receive training varying from two to six
months. Training courses will be repeated as necessary, thus most of the
workforce will be trained more than once depending on their ability to learn
and retain. The actual number of personnel to be trained will depend on
agreements reached by DNM/GAPROMAR on number and types of concessions
contracted to the private sector. It is estimated that over 200 personnel
ranging from managers and chief tallymen to crane/forklift operators and
mechanics will require training. However, since most personnel will attend
more than one training course, the cumulative total of personnel to be trained
would be considerably higher than 200 depending on the transfer of workforce
to other sectors and turnover (15-20X).
IV. The Trainint Progr
4.1        The main elements of the three-year training program are: (i)
training technical assistance; (ii) local port schools; (iii) in-country
regional ports; (iv) training abroad (fellowships); (v) training equipment and
materials; (vi) training allowance; and (vii) project supervision and
evaluation. DNH/GAPROMAR and ports' managers will attend in-country seminars
and workshops some of which are expected to be conducted by project technical
assistance and will also participate in short-term training courses abroad
(fellowships) at small ports whose activities correspond approximately to
those in Mozambique. Training of lower-level port personnel would be
conducted in-country at existing port schools and regional ports. Training or
upgrading of forklift drivers as well as other mobile equipment would include
familiarization with equipment in the mechanical workshops and practical
experience in driving and operating it in a purpose-built training area in
which driving hazards and obstacles encountered in the work area can be safely
simulated. Such training creates confidence in the trainee, especially when
learning to drive the more complex and expensive equipment, thereby reducing
the likelihood of accidents or damages in the work areas. Because of the low
level of education and literacy of most personnel, in addition to relevant
technical subjects, many trainees should also take more general subjects such
as Portuguese, English and arithmetic in order to strengthen their educational
backgrounds.
4.2         Training courses financed under ROCS-1 will be available to all
interested entities directly connected with the small ports and shipping
operations on a recurrent cost recovery basis. Numbers, categories and levels
of personnel expected to be trained under the project are detailed in
Attachments one and two of this Annex.



- 165 -
Annex 4-8
Page 3 of 3
V. Technical Assistance
5.1        One TA port training adviser will assist DNH/GAPROMAR and port
concessionaires with the preparation and implementation of the training
program. The adviser will be retained for a total of 36 months. After an
initial period of 21 months to organize and commence the program, the adviser
will visit Mozambique at agreed intervals to ensure that the program continues
to be properly implemented according to an agreed time schedule. A
counterpart will be assigned to the adviser from the very beginning to
familiarize himself with the program and monitor its implementation while the
adviser is away. Both the advisers and the counterpart will be located within
DN/IGAPROMAR Terms of Reference for the TA training adviser are available in
the Project File.
VI. TraininR Facilities
6.1        As training of skilled and semiskilled personnel will be conducted
at existing port schools and some of the regional ports, no additional
physical facilities will be provided under the training component.
VII. Trainins Zauitment and Haterials
7.1        The project provides funds for renting one tug boat to train seamen
and captains at the Nautlcal School (ENM) and for technical pualications and
books for the RPM and ZNM. Other training equipment is already available at
existing training facilities.
VII. Training Allowance
8 1        A daily training allowance will be provided under the project to
the counterpart of the training adviser (when on travel status) and trainees
attending regularly-scheduled training courses.
IX. Proiect Evaluation
9.1        The project provides financing for an independent evaluation of the
training program to be conducted at the end of the second year (late 1994) and
the end of the program (late 1996), including recommendations for further
training activities.
X. Cost Estimates
10.1       The total cost of the training program is estimated at about US$1.9
million equivalent as detailed in Attachments 3 and 4 to this Annez.

MOZAMBIOUD
FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
THE FINANCIAL SECTOR
1.01      General.  Mozambique's financial sector currently consists of
three banks, an agricultural credit fund, an investment and financial
studies association, and one insurance company. The present financial
system came into being after Mozambique gained its independence in 1975,
with the restructuring of the banking system in 1978. Of the three banks,
the Banco de Mocambique (BH) is predominant, accounting for some 95Z of
financial-system assets. It has operated both as the Central Bank and as
the leading commercial bank, although lending has been confined primarily
to parastatal borrowers. A second fully state-owned bank is Banco Popular
de Desenvolvimento (BPD). The third bank - Banco Standard Totta de
Mocambique (BSTM) - is a small, largely foreign-owned private bank which
lends to the private sector. The agricultural credit fund - Caiza de
Credito Agrario e de Desenvolvimento Rural (CCADR) - was established by
GoverAment in 1988, is administered by BPD and initiated lending operations
in mid-1988. The Sociedade de Investimentos e Estudos Financieros (SOCIEF)
handles GOM bond issues.
1.02      Over the period 1980-86 the banking system acted as a passive
conduit for financing the production plans of the centralized economy.
Physical controls over production, allocation and prices were installed and
resources were concentrated in large, capital-intensive agricultural and
industrial investments in the public sector. A rapid expansion of domestic
credit occurre'd so that while GDP fell in real terms by 16t over this
period, domestic credit expanded by 240t, and serious arrears were built up
amounting to over 50t of lending by 1986. Interest rates on lending and
borrowing were not used for rationing of credit and were negative in real
terms. Bank deposits were largely non interest-bearing demand deposits.
The situation was not conducive to mobilization of real savings and the
non-availability of foreign exchange for import of investment goods
constrained investment, so that liquid reserves were accumulated. Lending
was concentrated in the commercial department of the BM, partly financed by
deposits from the other banks; this was done particularly at the expense of
the private sector. The dual role of BM was not conducive to the
development of its regulatory and policy making responsibilities.
ComuercLal banks were able to maintain financial stability because of the
high levels of liquidity; book earnings were also positive because of high
yield margins over the average cost of deposited funds (and also possibly
because of accrued interest). However, lack of proper auditing resulted in
unreliable balance-sheet information, which, in turn, led to unsatisfactory
economic data and inappropriate banking policy decisions.
1.03      The Strategy for Financial Sector Reform. The Government, since
1987, has adopted a series of relatively radical measures for the banking
sector under its Economic Rehabilitation Program (ERP), with the objective
of restoring capability for efficient mobilization of savings and
allocation of investment. The pace of change implies a high level of
commitment. The measures taken have been included in agreements between
the Government, IDA, and the IMF. The task that the Government has
embarked on since 1987 is complex and lengthy, requiring that the financial



- 173 -
Annex 4-10
Page 2 of 6
sector undergo a transition from a system whose main purpose was the
administrative allocation of public funds according to the requirements of
the central plan, to one in which private savings can be effectively
mobilized and channelled to economic investment outlets via a market
determined price (interest rate) system, and which can be monitored and
regulated through monetary instruments by the central bank. Under the ERP,
the main instrument of monetary control has been the establishment of
credit ceilings which, until recently, have not been strictly applied.
This situation, combined with the persistence of high inflation, led the
GOM in 1991 to move to a system based on net domestic assets rather than
domestically sourced credit. In order to rehabilitate the financial
sector, the reform process has had to address the following basic areas:
(a) inappropriate interest and exchange rates; (b) the need for
institutional restructuring; (c) tightening of monetary and fiscal
policies; (d) restraint of wages and a shift towards market-determined
prices; and (e) establishment of financial-performance guidelines. The
combined effect of reforms in all these areas is expected to be to raise
domestic savings and ration credit via the price system to more profitable
outlets, allow proper execution of the functions of the central bank, and
permit the commercial banking system to become a catalyst for sustained
growth of the economy.
1.04      The ERP, reflected in the Government's Policy Framework Paper,
initially addressed (a) and (b) above. Under the program agreed with the
IHF, nominal interest rates have been increased in successive steps toward
positive real levels as the rate of price inflation declines. By the end
of 1991, the official exchange rate was reasonably close to secondary and
parallel-market rates. A System of Non-administrative Allocation of
Foreign Exchange (SNAAD) was introduced and is to be expanded (at the
secondary market rate) in 1992. There have been important reforms in
pricing and marketing, and domestic trade has been liberalized. Fiscal
imbalances have been addressed by widening the coverage of the budget,
reforming tax policy, and eliminating monetary financing of the budget;
expenditures have simultaneously been contained. Monetsry policy has aimed
at containing money supply growth by restraining credit expansion, while
interest rates have been progressively simplified and made positive in real
terms. This dramatic economic change has been accompanied by political
liberalization (including the privatization of state enterprises), the
promulgation of a new constitution in 1990, and planning for multi-party
elections in 1992. In the institutional area, the complex task of
separating the Bank of Mozambique (BH) into its central and commercial
functions has been initiated, under the 1991 reform of the general banking
act and the central banking act. This separation is needed to restore the
central bank's regulatory and monetary control functions, and its role as
financier of the central government (accounting separation began the second
half of 1991, but separated balance sheets are not yet available). In
addition, some initial progress has been made on diversification of
instruments of government finance and establishment of prudential
regulatory controls.
1.05      The rehabilitation of the financial system is now focussed on
interest rate reform and on institution building and reorganization. The
financial restructuring area is relatively less advanced, and the



- 174 -
Annex 4-10
Page 3 of 6
reintroduction of an effective regulatory system for the commercial banks
will to some extent require inter alia the prior achievement of progress in
restructuring and the final clearance of lending arrears, which, up to now,
has been confined to those of BM (para 1.09). The regulatory powers of the
BM have not been utilized because reserve requirements have not been
effective due to excess rediscount facility 1/. In addition,
regulations concerning financial performance and prudent lending have not
been effective because of the financing of enterprise deficits during the
period of central planning. The pace at which improvements can be made in
these areas depends to a large extent on the progress of institutional
reform in terms both of organization (of the BM), and in terms of eapacity
(skilled manpower) of all the banks. In particular, manpower constraints
in the commercial banks in the area of accounting, financial management and
credit analysis will take time to resolve, as well as skill shortages in
central banking functions. The February 1990 Small and Medium Enterprise
Development Project provides technical assistance and conditionality
designed to strengthen term lending capabilities of commercial banks.
1.06      Structure of Interest Rates.   Rates in effect from 1981 to 1986
were very low in nominal terms, ranging from 0 to 6Z for deposits, and 3 to
10X for lending, and negative in real terms. The structure of interest
rates has been revised six times under the ERP, with real rates becoming
less negative and segmented. The change in September 1991 reduced the
number of sectoral lending livels from three to two and further raised
nominal rates. Credit provicled from resources of the CCADR bears
preferential interest rates ranging from 3Z to 6Z p.a.
1.07      Under the terms of Mozambique's agreement with the IMF, interest
rates should continue to be reviewed and revised progressively. Annual
reviews of lending interest rates and their structure would also take place
with IDA. A high priority for GOM should be a rapid movement towards the
elimination of interest-rate segmentation and distortions within the
present rate soructure, on both lending and deposit schedules (e.g. the
current high premia of 29-33Z on time deposits over demand deposits is not
warranted). Banks should be allowed to determine lending rates based upon
a commercial assessment of anticipated risk and return. Although the scope
for domestic savings mobilization over the near term is severely limited,
such mobilization should become a more important macro-economic goal over
the medium term.
1.08      Banking System Arrears.  Despite the indications of positive
progress, the banking system still faces a serious arrears problem which
has arisen from several sources. A portion of the arrears dates back to
independence, when many Portuguese firm owners left the country; a number
of these firms were subsequently intervened by the Government. The bulk of
the arrears of the private bank, BSTM, date from this period. The largest
share of arrears, however, has stemmed from lending, largely to state
enterprises and intervened firms, by the banks (mainly DM and BPD) at the
Government's request. Much of this lending was to cover operating deficits
(up to 1986 inclusive) considered to be due to price controls and the
1/ These powers are codified under the Organic Law, decree 2/75.



- 175 -
Annex 4-10
Page 4 of 6
difficult economic environment facing the enterprises. Since 1987, the
Government has covered such deficits through direct .iscal subsidy and has
reduced their level substantially. However, some government subsidies
remain, in the form of subsidized negative real interest rates on some
types of borrowing, direct government assumption of operating losses of
some parastatal enterprises, and continued government guarantees on
borrowing through the banking system. Such activities should either be
gradually eliminated or passed directly to the GOM budget.
1.09      The consolidation of the bad loans of public enterprises and
their transfer from the banking sector to the Government has been under
negotiation since 1986. Government has recognized MT 40.6 billion of BM's
portfolio as bad loans of the public enterprises, as of the end of 1987.
Of these, about MT 10.0 billion have been rescheduled and a portion of the
balance has already been reimbursed to BM by the Government. The two other
banks are conducting similar negotiations with Government; this will be
especially significant for BPD which has accumulated large arrears from the
state farm sector and it is estimated that Government would assume
responsibility for about MT 20.0 billion of BPD's portfolio.
1.10      The IDA Credit for the Small and Medium Enterprise Development
Project contains agreements on the actions to be taken by the Government
leading to: (a) the separation of BM's central and commercial Bank
functions (substantially completed by April 1992); (b) the settlement of
lending arrears of the commercial banks including BM; (c) the
rehabilitation of the banking system and extension of competition within
the banking sector; and (d) the strengthening of the accounting
capabilities of the PFIs. As indicated above, measures are being taken to
implement the agreed actions.
Banco de Mocambigue (BM).
1.11      Organization.  EM is by far the largest of the three banks, and
the one with the broadest mandate. It was created in 1975 to take over the
central banking role previously played by Portugal's Banco Nacional
Ultramarino, and, in 1978, absorbed the Mozambican operations of four
Portguguese-owned banks. BM has 10 provincial head offices (filiais), 17
district branches (agencias) and 13 deposit offices (delegacoes). BM's
Board of Directors is entirely internally constituted, comprising the
Governor and the eight department General Managers (Administradores).
1.12      Policies and Functions.  Under new legislation, BM is expected
to exercise control over monetary policy through indirect macro-economic
management rather than through direct forms of micro-economic control. The
bank's most important policy instrument has been the interest-rate policy,
with BM and the Ministry of Finance setting a complex structure of lending
and deposit rates. The government has begun to move towards a more
deregulated system of monetary policy control; however, until there is
sufficient competition in the banking system, BM will need to set minimum
lending rates to ensure that they remain positive in real terms.
1.13      The Bank of Mozambique needs to establish a strong
administrative structure to negotiate and manage its large external debt.



- 176 -
Annex 4-10
Page 5 of 6
Alternatives such as debt conversions should be pursued by BM, and the bank
should also improve its statistical base on this debt. The foreign
exchange and trade-permit system should be streamlined, with all banks
being permitted to effect foreign exchange transactions. However, foreign-
exchange management should be centralized with BM and a strong control unit
developed with the aid of external technical assistance. Another important
function which should be developed is the ability to properly supervise and
regulate banking and financial institutions. BM must develop this
capability quickly, with the aid of external technical assistance and a
contingent of good calibre local staff.
The Commercial BankinR Sector.
1.14      The Banco Popular de Desenvolvimento.  BPD was formed in 1977,
integrating the old Banco de Fomento Nacional, Instituto de Credito de
Mocambiqua, and Montepio de Mocambique.  It has traditionally had two main
roles: the financing of agriculture and, by extension, the rural sector,
and domestic savings mobilization. However, it is also empowered under its
charter to invest in equity, new ventures, and take up commercial paper.
This latter role has not been fulfilled, but recently a share capital
participation department was set up for that purpose. BPD also holds
equity in a few enterprises, notably textiles, inherited from nationalized
banks. It is 10O state-owned and has by far the largest branch network in
the country, with 10 provincial offices (filial), 28 district branches
(agencia), and 120 deposit offices (delegacao) and savings collection posts
(posto de captacao de poupanca). BPD has a total staff of about 3,000 with
420 employed in its head office in Maputo. Since 1987 BPD management has
considerably strengthened its staff training activities, focussing in
particular on training of credit analysts, and is gradually introducing
computerization in head office departments and some of the larger branches.
1.15      BPD's balance sheet has shown considerable excess liquidity in
recent years, largely because its savings mobilization role was combined
with the lack of sufficient short-term lending opportunities. As of
December 31, 1988, liquid assets were about 36Z of total assets and BPD's
total loan portfolio was equivalent to 55Z of demand and term deposits, or
38Z when loans to Government and the CCADR are excluded. The outstanding
balance of loans (including arrears) at that time amounted to MT 41.4
billion, primarily composed of loans to agricultural state enterprises.
However, lending by BPD to the private sector started to gain momentum in
1988 and an analysis of current BPD lending activities shows a significant
change in the composition of the portfolio resulting from increasing
lending to the private sector for investments as well as for working
capital (the *healthy* portfolio has increased from 13Z to 33?).
1.16      As already mentioned in para 1.08, BPD faces severe arrears
problems. Available data show that at the end of December 1988, arrears
amounted to MT 26.6 million or 652 of BPD's total loan portfolio as of that
date, and state enterprises accounted for about 92Z of arrears. BPD is
negotiating with Government to resolve the problem of bad loans and to
determine the amount to be reimbursed by the Government. Recent data also
indicate that recovery on current lending is improving. BPD is in the



- 177 -
Annex 4-10
Page 6 of 6
process of introducing better loan recovery monitoring and follow-up
procedures which would assist it in its loan collection efforts.
1.17      Caixa de Credito Aprario e de Desenvolvimento Rural (CCADR).  rhe
CCADR, in operation since Hay 1988 and administered by BPD, was created by
Government to provide credit at preferential terms and at subsidized
interest raues (currently 102 p.a.) to stimulate investments in the rural
sector. Its activities are financed from budgetary allocations by
Government and the local counterpart funds of some donor-financed projects.
Although state enterprises are eligible to borrow from the CCADR, the
credit norms for its operations emphasize lending to veterans, repatriated
migrant workers, cooperatives and family producers, producers of cereals
and those affected by natural calamities. By September 1990, the CCADR had
accumulated a loan portfolio of HT 32.0 billion, consisting mainly (90X) of
medium and long-term loans for in'7estments. However, all new lending has
been suspended because available funds have been exhausted.
1.18      Necessary Reforms.  The commercial banking sector in Hozambique
is in urgent need of rehabilitation and restructuring; the rehabilitation
is strongly linked to the restructuring and divestment of inefficient and
loss-producing state enterprises. Banking legislation should ensure that:
5i) banks have an adequate capital base; (ii) an appropriate licensing
procedure is in effect to reasonably limit ownership in any one bank; (iii)
lending concentrations are restricted to avoid over-exposure to a single
group or area of the economy; (iv) adequate supervision by the eentral bank
is carried out; (v) proper accounting, auditing and reporting are
conducted; tvi) entry of foreign banks is permitted; and (vii) a Bankers'
Training Institute is established and properly staffed and funted.
1.19      improvements must be made in the expeditious transfer of funds
and the clearance of cheques. It is especially important to take measures
to restore confidence in the use of the cheque system, including the
initiation of criminal procedures against those who abuse the system.
Clearing systems between banks and their branches must be radically
improved, possibly through a two-level system, at the provincial level and
thereafter in Haputo. Given the existing low levels of capital in the
state-owned banks, GOH should consider divesting itself completely of BPD
(at the end of 1991, a German team was working on possible restructuring
and reorientation of BPD), and either phasing out the commercial wing of BH
or selling a minority interest to the private sector. Conditions should
also be created to permit the issuance of money market instruments in more
standardized and easily understandable forms, as well as the issuance of
bank certificates of deposit.
1.20      The development of adequate banking skills at all levels is
crucial. The magnitude of required training is enormous and pervasive
throughout all levels of expertise and in all financial institutions.
Computerization should also be adopted as rapidly as personnel skills can
accomodate it.
1.21      Additional details relative to the financial sector of
Mozambique may be found in the April 1992 Financial Sector Study (Report
No. 10269-MOZ).

IDMPLEMNTATION ARRANGEMENTS FOR THE CREDIT LINE
A. Participation Agreement
1.        Participation Agreements between BH/Central and the
Participating Financial Institutions (PFI) would, inter alia, specify that
the PFIs shoulds
(i)    designate qualified staff to manage the  sub-loans
financed by the proposed project;
(ii)   perform satisfactory sub-project appraisals and submit
appraisal reports and required related documentation to
DM/Central;
(iii)  ensure that sub-loan funds are used by sub-project
beneficiaries for the purposes intended and that
procurement procedures, acceptable to IDA, are followed;
(iv)   supervise sub-projects according to established
procedures, maintain adequate records and provide periodic
reports on the status of sub-projects and sub-loans;
(v)    bear the lending risk and adhere to terms of lending and
repayment of loans;
(vi)   provide BM/Central, GAPROMAR and IDA with such information
as they may reasonably requestg and
(vii)  provide, from their own resources, short-term credits to
cover incremental working capital requirements of project
beneficiaries.
B. Sub-proiect Selection and Eliaibility Criteria
2.        In order to receive financing from the line of credit, analysis
of the followlng aspects would have to prove satisfactorily:
(i)    the credit-worthiness of the prospective sub-borrower;
(iL)  the technical, financial, environmental and economic
viability of the proposed investments;
(1ii)  the financial rate of return (FRR) of proposed investment
sub-projects would be not lower than 12 percent;



- 179 -
Annex 4-11
Page 2 of 2
C. Repayment of Funds Borrowed from the Line of Credit
3.        PFIs will be required to repay to BM/Central the funds on-lent
from the line of credit in accordance with the repayment schedules
established for the individual sub-loans made by PFIs, even if PFIs have
not collected the amounts in question on schedule. PFIs would pay to
BM/Central, semi-annually or annually, interest on the funds/borrowed from
the line of credit equivalent to 70 percent of the amount of interest
accrued on sub-loans financed from the ltne of credit. The spread for
PFIs, equivalent to 30S of in,;erest on sub-loans reflects the relatively
high administrative costs and the need to ensure adequate coverage of the
lending risks borne by PFIs.
4.        DM/Central would reimburse to the Government the amounts of
principal and interest as and when collected from PFus, less a fee of 0.5?
p.a. of the amount of outstanding principal, to cover the administrative
costs of BM/Central. The amount of interest paid to the Government would
cover the commitment and service charges on the IDA credit and make
provision for coverage of the foreign exchange risk.

MOZAMBIQUE
FIRST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
RzGULATORY AND SUPERVISION - DRAFT TRMS OF REPRINCE
Introduction:
1.    The Government of Mozambique (GO), recognizing the important role
that the coastal shipping sector plays in the distribution of agricultural
produce in Mozambique. is embarking on a program (the ROCS project) to
increase efficiency in, and attract private sector investments to, the
shipping and small ports industries.
2.    The small ports and coastal shipping recovery strategy involves: (i)
reforming the regulatory environment; (ii) encouraging the private sector
operators; (iii) long term training and operational assistance; and (iv)
phased investment in shipping and ports. More specifically, the recovery
strategy includes:
(a)   Deregulating the industry to allow commercial operation and new
private sector entry, while maintaining supervision
responsibilities. GOM has agreed to adopt and promulgate a
firm policy cnauitment to promote and maintain full
competitiveness in the coastal shipping transport sub-sector.
In addition an action plan for implementation of cost-based
tariff policies for the coastal shipping sub-sector has been
prepared.
(b)   Restructuring of largely state-owned shipping enterprises into
viable commercial entities. Re-equipment of the coastal
shipping fleet.
(c)   Rehabilitation of the small-port infrastructure and equipment
to allow shorter port-side waiting times for ships.
(d)   Re-orientation of the Ministry of Commuications and Transport
into a planning and supervisory organization.
Overall Obiectivest
3.    To support this effort GOM, with financing from IDA, will contract
the services of a consulting firm to assist with all aspects of regulatory
reform and supervision of physical works at selected small ports and for
the coastal shipping industry in Mosambique. The overall objectives of this
assignment are:
Shipping Industry: To assist DNM by: (i) revising the regulatory
framework for shipping, covering shipping concessions, competition
and new entry, tariff setting and safety; (ii) advising GOM in
negotiating the privatization of Navique and any potential new
entries; and (iii) establishing improved practices for the longer
term supervision of and planning for the coastal shipping industry,
and transferring management skills to local counterpart staff.



- 181 -
Annex 4-12
Page 2 of 10
Small Ports: To assist DNH byt (i) revising the regulatory framework
for small ports to allow concessions and cost based tariffs; (ii)
finalizing the contractual details of, and assisting tne Government
in negotiating, the concessions for small port administration; and
(iii) establishing appropriate practices for the long term
supervision of and planning for the small ports.
Organizations To work with DNH and HTC to develop both short and long
term organization structures that will enable the Government of
Hozambique to manage the coastal shipping sector in years to come.
4.    the work assignment will cover four distinct areast
(a)   The initial changes to the regulatory framework
(b)   Ongoing supervision of the industry, including the transfer of
skills to Hozambican counterparts
(c)   Review of ongoing shipping and ports performance
(d)   Planning for the future of the coastal shipping industry
Scope of Work:
5.     The detailed scope of the work to be performed is as follows:
A.    SHIPPING
Task Ones  To review, and advise GOH on desirable revisions to, the
regulatory framework for the coastal shipping industry covering shipping
concessions. new entry, and tariff setting.
1.1   Review of current leRislation:
6.   Under separate funding a consultaut has been engaged to pull together
and review all relevant legislation concerning the coastal shipping sector
and has identified the main issues and obstacles relating to the operation
of an efficient and competitive coastal shipping industry. This analysis
will form the basis for the regulatory reform assignment and will be
reviewed by the Consultant at the start of the assignment.
1.2   Define New Shipping Environment:
7.    It is generally agreed that a alevel playing field  (equal
conditions) is required for a healthy shipping industry. All the potential
players in the coastal shipping markets are concerned that the same rights
and obligations be assigned to all participants, or that they be
compensated accordingly. The Consultant will be responsible for advising
GQM on the structure of such a regulatory framework. The new framework
shoald be able to deal with the following:
(a)   There is concern that if market entry and participation is
entirely free, new entrants will be attracted only to the lucrative
core toutes. The lower-demand, less profitable routes will then
either be faced with a dramatic reduction in service levels, or those
that are forced to operate such routes will be at a comnercial
disadvantage. The revised regulatory framework will need to establish
conditions for entry intc the market and conditions and compensation
arrangements for those required to serve less profitable routes.



- 182 
Annex 4-12
Page 3 of 10
(b)   It still is not clear whether the Mozambican market can support
the existence of more than one coastal shipping organization. Many
believe, for example, that the proposal by French shipping interests
together with the proposed re-equipment of NAVIQUE would result in
excess capacity and that unrestricted competition would result in a
shake-out in the market and eventually in a monopoly. The regulatory
framework will need to develop an appropriate way of dealing with
this challenge. Just as shipping regulations would have to ensure
that all operators have a 'level playing field", the Consultant will
be responsible for 'dvising the governuent on ways of protecting the
rights of Mozambican shippers from the problems that might arise due
to the development of a monopoly.
(c)   The framework should clearly address the question of
concessional finance for the participants in the coastal shipping
sector. Should concessional donor funding be allowed and, if so, how
should it be controlled, if at all? One solution to be considered is
an arrangement under which all donor funding to the shipping sector
be channeled through the central bank and onward to the shipping
entities at commercial rates. The concessional element of the funding
could then be retained by the Government to support the development
of the coastal shipping industry and to finance subsidies for
particular routes (if required).
(d)   The new regulatory framework should address the structure and
level of freight rates. The Government has agreed in principle to
allow cost-based freight rates with 'automatic adjustments' to be
introduced into the coastal shipping sector. An initial level and
structure of rates should be proposed. Options should be set out and
recommendations made regarding the denomination of rates (Dollars or
Mfeticais), any differential between products, and mechanisms for rate
adjustment.
8.    Output of this part of the work will be a policy statement to be
adopted by GOM and accepted by the major shipping companies. Based on the
legislative review and the revised competitive framework, the Consultant
will redraft the legislation required to allow the new policy to be
implemented.
9.    The Consultant will not responsible for legislation and policy
regarding maritime safety, since this is currently being implemented under
NORAD assistance to DNM. However the Consultant will be expected to liaise
with those working in the field of maritime safety and will need to ensure
that recosended initiatives are compatible with each other.
10.   The Consultant will have responsibility for discussing the proposed
new regulatory framework with shipping entities active in Mozambique, as
well as with those discussing with GOM their possible participation.
Task Two: To advise GMI by reviewing progress towards, and assisting in
negotiations for. the privatization of Navique or its joint venture with
any potential new entries In the coastal shipping field in Mozambique.

Annex 4-12

2.1   Review Progress towards and Possible Negotiation of a Joint Venture
for Naviaues
11. A formal restructuring plan for NAVIQUE is being prepared by GOM,
which is expected to lay out clearly monitorable targets and deadlines, and
to cover both 'external restructuring', such as changes in the ownership
structure and holding of shares, and 'internal restructuring', such as
staff and fleet rationalization. The Consultant will review the progress
made by Navique towards more commercial operation and ownership and will
review the suitability of its proposed restructuring plan. The Consultant
will also advise and assist GOM with implementation of actions which the
government decides to take to privatize Navique. The Consultant will not be
involved in the day-to-day management or restructuring of Navique itself.
which is covered by NORAD assistance, but will focus on advising the
government on the merits and possible disadvantages of any proposed
restructuring of Navique.
12.   It is clear that without staff rationalization and financial
restructuring it will be very difficult to interest a private sector
organization in acquiring NAVIQUE. IDA and NORAD have expressed
willingness to try to find ways of using cr %dits or grants to assist in the
commercial restructuring of NAVIQUE (under the ROCS II project) in these
two areas. The Consultant will advise GOM on options for dealing with
these and other issues leading up the possible privatization of Navique. In
all likelihood Navique will need to be trimmed down, with 'good' assets
moving to the 'neww Navique and unwanted assets, excessive liabilities and
redundant staff to be dealt with by MTC. The Consultant would be expected
to advise GOM on its options for dealing with such an eventuality. This
will require : 5i) legal advice; (ii) agreement on the most advantageous
restructuring path for NAVIQUE; and (iii) a detailed understanding of
NAVIQUE's asset and liability structure. The specialist services of an
investment banking firm might be required to help structure such a deal.
13.  When the time comes to negotiate a joint venture or the full
privatization of Navique the Consultant will be expected to assist the
government in negotiating favorable arrangements.
2.2   Negotiate New Entry:

act as advisor to the Government of Mozambique in negotiating the entry of
such companies, in line with requirements of the new regulatory framework.

D.    PORTS
19.   The strategy for recovery of the small ports calls for: (i) changing
the regulations to allow private sector operators to manage the small ports
in a commercial mnanner: (ii) awarding concessions for the management of
the ports; (iii) facilitating the rehabilitation and re-equipment or
development of the ports; and (iv) supporting the capacity development of
the small ports to allow the operators to manage the facilities and the
government to properly regulate the industry.
20.   In order to implement this strategy it will be necessary to change
regulations to allow the adoption of cost related tariffs for small-port
operations and to issue contracts for management and maintenance
(concessions) of the small ports, on a commercial basis, to independent
operators.
Task Siz: To advise OoK on required revisions to the regulatory framework
for small ports to allow use of concessions and cost based tariffs and to
Implawt such revisions as are adopted by GM3

6.1 Review of Current Regulatory Structures
21. The small ports are currently owned and, in some cases, operated by
CPM. This is not an effective arrangement in the long term. MTC is
comiitted to granting concessions for the operation of tertiary ports to
independent operators, and CRH is willing to relinquish operational
responsibility to current or potential users. Clearly the regulatory
structure will need to be revised to allow this to happen.
22. A review of the current regulatory structure of the small ports is
already underway. This will look at the legal ownership of the small
ports, the basis for granting concessions and the basis for establishing
port handling fees. The Consultant will be expected to review this
analysis as a first step towards the restructuring of the regulatory
framework for the small ports.
6.2   Define the New Regulatory Structures
23.   The Consultant will be expected to advise GOH on the structure of
appropriate concession agreements for the management of the small ports and
on the ultimate ownership of the ports themselves.
24.   Alternatives are for the ownership to rest with HTC/GAPROMAR, later
with the local governments, or ultimately with the concessionaire. The
final ownership issue needs to be agreed upon before the concessions are
awarded. It has been tentatively agreed that ownership and responsibility
for the small (tertiary) ports will be formally transferred from CRH to
DNH/GAPROMAR with appropriate provisions to grant long-term concessions to
main users or experienced operators.
25. The Consultant will advise DNH/GAPROMAR on the development of an
action plan for implementation of cost-based tariff policies for the
coastal shipping sub-sector. The Consultant will also advise on an
appropriate structure for port handling fees, including questions of
adjustment mechanisms and the possibility of charging some fees in hard
currency. Possibilities for allowing differential fees for different
products will also need to be explored.
6.3 Drafting Concession Documents:
26. The Consultant will be required to draft concession documents,
consistent with the revised regulatory framework, setting out the rights
and obligations of potential concession holders. These documents should
cover at least the following pointst
-     responsibilities for maintenance
-     arrangements for, and structure and method of calculation of, the
concession fees.
-     arrangements for and structure of fees that may be charged to port
users, including regulations with respect to fee adjustments and
discrimination between products.
-     enforcement arrangements
-     decision making responsibilities with respect to capital investments

7.1   Negotiation of Concession Agreements
27.   While negotiating the concession contracts will be a challenge, it is
believed that suitable concessionaires can be identified and suitable
agretments structured on a port-by-port basis. The Consultant will be
responsible for advising GOM in this regard. Potential concessionaires may
include users, shipping companies or joint ventures between various
interested parties. Municipalities and provincial governments are also
potential operators. Concessions may be awarded for the operation of
multiple ports within a region (e.g. Moma and Angoche) to the same
concessionaire to allow benefits to be obtained from economies of scale.
The Consu'tant will be expected to advise GOM on the best method for
awarding the concessions.  To ensure trnsparency, open advertising for
bids for the award of concessions would be preferable.
28.   The structure of the concession fee will need to be negotiated with
interested parties such thats (i) the ports themselves vill be financially
viable; (ti) MTC will recover its investment costs plus interest; and (iii)
overall fees should be acceptable to shippers taking into account the
improved service that they will receive.

the longer term these separate responsibilities will need to be reconciled.

MOAMBIQUE
P - QADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
Transit Logtintic. d Customs Facilitation - Proposed Action Program
1.    Facilitation of domestic and international freight transfers through
regional and small ports requires a coherent approach to the various
institutions involved and to the private sector. None of the issues
identified in Annex 3-6 can properly be dealt with separately. The
proposed actions will be combined into an 'interutodal facilitation program
divided into two different parts2 (i) freight transfer logistics; and (ii)
customs. There will be three different stages of implementation/execution.
2.    Freight transfer logistics:  o         the logistic chain to private
sector investment means providing a positively free regulatory environment
where shippers and carriers, or their agents, interact. This means
deregulating the current environment and opening stevedoring, port-
handling, freight-forwarding, customs brokerage, and in general, transport
intermediaries professions to comercial operation. The following program
components have been identified:
(i)   facilitating documents and procedures in the import/export
documentary credit process to international layout key
standards (document facilitation)l
(ii)  revising the existing freight-forwarding regulatory framework
to facilitate private access to the transport intermediaries
professions while encouraging domestic entries and
participation;
(iii) defining modern standards of liability for transport and
technical operators by up-dating and redrafting terms and
conditions of carriage and terminal operators liability
standards to meet international container transport
requirements; and
(iv)  reviewing insurance regulatory provisions to ensure
competitiveness and freedom of choice on import/export
insurance coverages while encouraging local corpanies
participation in the international insurance market.
3.    Close coordination between the execution of the freight-transfer
logistics part of the intermodal facilitation program, and the ongoing
liberalization initiative of stevedoring and port-handling operations, will
be essential.
4.    Customs facilitation and modernizations  the second part of the
intermodal facilitation program should focus on closing the gap between
current UNCTAD-executed projects RAF/86/046 (regional SATCC project) and
MOZ/89/018, both containing assistance to the customs administration at the
regional and domestic levels. In particular, the customs facilitation
program should target the following areass



- 191 -
Annex 4-13
Page 2 of 3
(i)   facilitation of customs documents and procedures for
importlexport trade and for domestic coastal shipping
transfers; and
(ii) supporting the introduction of electronic data processing in
the cv3toms administration by providing complementary
assistance, and preparing the way for potential communications
improvements such as EDI?ACT (electronic data information for
administration, commerce, and transport).
5.    The intermodal facilitation program should be implemented in three
separate str 2a:
*     first, definition of documents, regulations, and procedures to
be facilitated, determination of intervening institutions and
critical path for consequent administrative changes;
*     second, preparation/drafting of new documents, regulations, and
procedures; proposals to carry out proposed administrative
changes through institutioonal/executive channels; and
*     third, implementation and execution of proposals.
6.    Implementation of the proposed intermodal logistics program (third
stage) would follow-up the ROCS-1 project implementation and be financed
under KOCS-2. Execution of the first and second stages of the program
could be completed in one and one-half years, each stage lasting for
approximately six months with about three months between stages and three
months as a contingency period. UNCTAD could be selected by GOH as the
Executing Agency for the intermodal logistics program. UNCTAD of Geneva
has the capabilities to provide expertise on customs facilitation (PALPRO)
electronic data comaunications (ASYCUDA) and freight-forwarding (Shipping
Division) under one roof.
7.    The transport and trade facilitation committees  The coordination of
the proposed intermodal logistic facilitation program at the local level
should be through a committee (the Transport and Trade Facilitation
Committee). The Bank and UNCTAD would have a seat in the quarterly
committee meetings as observers.  The proposed committee would be formed of
representatives of the public sector enterprises and private sector
companies engaged in transport and trade (banks, customs, insurance, public
trans;ort enterprises, freight forwarders, transport operators, etc.). The
committee would be the natural channel of communication between GOM and the
operators. It would channel and expedite documents to be facilitated or
regulations to be approved through GOM administration and should have
authority to do so. SimLilar committees have proved to be of enormous value
to introduce administratLve reform in countries like Ivory Coast
(competitiveness Committee reporting to the Prime Minister) and Ethiopia.
The transport and trade facilitation committee would have a sub-sectoral
group dealing with road transport issues.

FIRS! ROADS AND COASTAL SUPPING PROJECT
MONI?P0r3 OPF TECNICAL ASSISTANCE
1.    ROC8 Includes a significant amount of technical assistance (TA)
aimed at Improving the institutional capacity of DNH/DNEP. Actually
achieving institutional strengthening through TA is a difficult task.
Bank ezperience in this area suggests that one of the most important
element of successful TA is an appropriate and effective monitoring
system.   Without this true institution building will not take place.
2.   Attached are oom  sample forms that might form the basis for such
a monitoring system. Each TA consultant will complete the forms on a
quarterly basis, will discuss this with both counterparts and
supervisors and all three will then sign off on the completed form.
This discussion in and of itself should bring significant results.
3.   Using Lotus and Word Perfect software the results of all reviews
may be compiled into a simple TA report within DNHIDNEP, for the
Director's review. A summary of TA performance could also then be
included in the quarterly report to donors.
4.    Exact details of what this entails would need to be worked out
within DNM1/DNP and with the donors, and finalized at the project launch
workshop scheduled for around November 1992. Likewise the reporting
system will give useful input into the ROCS mid-tern review scheduled
for October 1995.

MOLAMBIQUC
ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
PROJECT SUPERVISION PLAN
1.    It is envisaged that considerable multi-disciplinary resources
will be required to supervise this project in the light of the very weak
implementation capacity in Hozambique and the uncertainties that will
have to be faced by the project. To overcome these difficultiest (i)
specific supervision responsibilities will be written into the terms of
reference for the general consultants to both the roads and shipping
sub-sectors; (ii) arrangements will be made to involve other donors in
the supervision process as far as possible; and (iii) IDA will commit
significant resources to supervision.
2.    IDA Supervision Input:  Bank staff or consultant input into the
supervision of the project will amount to about 45 staff weeks for the
first year; about 30-35 staff weeks per annum for the next two years;
about 25 staff weeks thereafter, and will take the following forms
(a)   Portfolio Management Operationst The review of procurement
documents, progress reports and financial statements at
headquarters and correspondence and internal reporting
related to such activities are estimated to require about
15 staff weeks in the first year of the project, about 12
staff weeks in the second year, and about 10 staff weeks per
annum thereafter.
(b)   Field Supervision: It is planned to undertake field
supervision missions approximately three times per annum for
the first three years of the project and on a semi-annual
basis thereafter. The activity plan for the first three
years is shown below. The supervision of the project will
cover the following broad aspects:
(i)   Regulatory Climate:  Much of the project is aimed at
improving the regulatory environment. Reviewing the
work of the various TA consultants and ensuring that
the different recommended regulatory reforms are e
satisfactory and consistent with each other will
absorb significant resources.
(ii) Proiect Implementation: Because of the nature of the
project (software) frequent supervision missions will
be required for the entire five-year period.
(iii) Donor Co-ordination: About two meetings per annum
will be required with donors to ensure that all donors
are working harmoniously towards the same common goal

Pan 2 of 4
3.    Other Donors' Supervision In2uts
A mechanism vill be developed to allow donors to coordinate their
supervision activities. Significant use will need to be mad of
trust funds. Attempts will be made to get donors to fund
consultants for supervision from other sources.
4.    Borrower's Supervision Inputs
(a)   DNEP and GAPROHAR will provide IDA with quarterly
progresu reports in the format agreed upon and written
into the terms of referetice of their TA consultants.
(b)   All procurement documents submitted to the Bank will be
based on the adopted standard documents as soon as this is
possible. All procurement evaluations submitted to IDA for
review vill be accompanied by a cover note setting out all
the information required for review and other internal IDA
reporting requirements (eg. Form 384) in the summary form
agreed between GOM and IDA.
(c)   DNEP and GAPROMAR vill provide IDA with annual audit reports
of the project special accounts, in the format and according
to the time schedule specified in the credit agreement.
(d)   Three weeks prior to each mission, IDA will submit data
requirements for review during the mission to GAPROMAR and
DNEP, which will be prepared for presentation at a briefing
meeting at the start of the mission. Each mission will
include a coordination meeting with the directors of DNEP,
GAPROMAR and Banco da Mozambique and officials from the
relevant ministries. Otherwise, supervision briefings and
wrap-up meetings will be restricted to either the roads or
the coastal-shipping sub-sector.
(e)   In the third quarter of 1995, DNEP and GAPROMAR will prepare
for review by the Government and IDA a detailed analysis of
progress in implementing all aspects of the project (the
mid-term review).

once the general consultants take up their positions.

addition the following will be included:
(a) The Technical Assistance Aeports (see annex 4-17).

(d) Procurement/ Disbursement
(e) Design
(f) Contracts
(g) Quality Control

(i) Cash Flow (including diagram)
(j)   Comments and Recommendations.

and two of the attachment. These will be circulate to the management
team in each implementing unit and discussed and a monthly meeting,

FST ROADS AND COASTAL SHIPPING PROJECT
TSCNIICAL ASSISTANCE - TRANSPFR OF EXPETISE GUIDELINES
Introduction
1.        The  primary  reason  for  employing  technical  assistance  (TA)
personnel is to compensate for gaps or shortages in expertise to achieve
project objectives.  To ensure project sustainability, institutional and
local personnel performance improvement and, therefore, effectiveness of TA,
it is essential that the expertise brought in by the TA is systematically
transferred and imparted to the local associate personnel during the day-to-
day operations of project implementation involving the TA. This, in turn,
requires a well-defined, executed and monitored/evaluated TA component with
all involved (i.e. TA personnel, local associate personnel, supervisors and
sponsors/donors) having the same understanding and appreciation of the role
and responsibilities of all the main players, particularly the TA and local
associate personnel. Therefore, the TA components need to be well-designed
to ensure that the Terms of Reference adequately cover the aforementioned
elements necessary to ensure effectiveness of TA. To assist in such design
of TA components in the Roads and Coastal Shipping Project (ROCS), the
following guidelines are presented.
Obiectives.
2.        (a)      Global Obiectives of TA.  TA is generally employed to
provide the local associate and institution personnel with
new or improved systems and methodologies to enable
Improved and efficient performance during and after the
time of TA. Thus the general role of TA personnel is to:
(i)     develop efficient and effective management and
information/reporting systems for execution of
responsibilities of the institution or department
at which the TA is employed;
(ii)    impart to the local associates new or improved
methodologies;
(iii)   put   into   operation   the   new   or   improved
methodologies    and    management    and
information/reporting systems and obtain desired
results. This should be done in such a va.y that
the local associates are fully involved and are,
before the end of the TA engagement, able to
continue the application of the systems and
methodologies.
(b)     Terms of Reference.  Terms of Reference specific to the
area of requirement of TA must be prepared and agreed by
the contracting parties before the engagement of the TA
personnel. In addition to clearly defining the specific



- 211 -
Annex 4-20
Page 2 of 4
responsibilities of the concerned institutions/departments
as well as TA and local personnel, the Terms of Reference
must fulfill the global objectives of TA as described in
paragraph 2 (a) above.
Implementation of TA Programme.
3.        Transfer of expertise from TA personnel to local associates is best
achieved through a team work or participatory approach in which the TA
personnel work closely with and through the local associate personnel to
implement or accomplish the different specific tasks.   It is therefore
important to ensure that the TA personnel and local associates. are able to
actually work together. In order to make sure that this is so, the following
implementation guidelines are provided to be used for TA programmes.
(a)    Local associates.  Each TA expert should be assigned to
work with and through one or more local associates. The
associates assigned to work with the TA expert should have
basic knowledge and capacity to understand and absorb the
expertise made available by the TA personnel. These local
associates must be assigned and made aware of their roles
prior to the arrival of the TA erpert.   The designated
local associates should be available at all times during
the TA programme.   However,  in cases where personnel
reassignment is necessary (for example, important transfers
or necessary study leaves), it is necessary to ensure that
the local associate personnel situation is maintained at a
level that is required to enable achievement of the
projected results as defined in the terms of reference.
(b)     The TA experts.  Prior to engagement, the TA experts must
be given the terms of reference and show clear
understanding and interpretation of the terms of reference,
particularly regarding roles and responsibilities, working
relationships,  reporting and accountability.   In this
regard, the TA experts should have the same understanding
and interpretation of the terms of reference as the
employers.
(c)     Phasing out of TA personnel.  It is imperative that all
parties agree to a program of gradual phasing out of TA
responsibility. Too often, TA contracts are geared to a
time frame such as two or three years, without any
substantive provision for target dates for full assumption
of job responsibilities by local staff.
(d)     Operational guidelines.  In order to facilitate continuous
assessment of the performance of the TA program, the
following operational actions need to be takent
Mi)     I=mediately upon arrival to the project, each TA
expert should prepare:



- 212 -
Annex 4-20
Page 3 of 4
-   a list of specific tasks that are required to
carry out the job assignment as generally
defined by the terms of reference;
-   a detailed work plan or action program (with
monthly or weekly activities) to implement
tasks, and specifying dates of completion of
the tasks and the outputs thereof; and
- a description of the roles of the local
associates vis-a-vis those of the TA expert in
carrying out the specified tasks.   The TA
expert should also indicate how he/she intends
to work with the local associates to implement
the work plan.
(ii)    The list of tasks and the associated plan of
action should be reviewed and approved by the
Supervisor of the particular TA program.   This
supervisor will normally be the Head of the
department or institution receiving the TA, and
will supervise both the TA expert and the local
associates.
(iii)   The list of tasks and the plan of action may be
modified from time to time in accordance with
changes  in  the   demands   and   the  working
environment, but they should always provide
opportunities for attaining realistic progress in
the transfer of expertise.
Evaluation
4.        As noted above, a program of transfer of expertise by the TA expert
must be clearly defined. This transfer of expertise shall be given equal
consideration along with technical and professional factors in evaluating the
performance of each TA expert.   In order to facilitate the necessary
evaluation, the following actions will need to be taken.
(a)    periodic progress  reports  (monthly/quarterly)  will  be
prepared by TA experts to indicate:
Mi)     status of implementation of the specific tasks;
(ii)    results or outputs in accordance with the work
plan;
(iii)   role  of  personnel   (TA  experts   and  local
associates) in carrying out specific tasks;
(iv)    ability of the local associates to perform job
tasks efficiently and without supervision of the
TA expert.;



- 213 -
Annex 4-20
Page 4 of 4
(v)     specific expertise imparted and the level at which
it was understood by the local associate;
(vi)    specific problems or experiences relevant to the
implementation of the work plan.
(b)     The supervisor will review the reports, verify contents and
take necessary actions, including measures to improve or
consolidate the working relationships between the TA expert
and local associates.
(c)     The supervisor will transmit to higher management the TA
expert's report and his comments regarding progress of
implementation of the TA program.
(d)     Higher management will issue a brief annual evaluation of
each TA expert's performance which will be sent to the
respective funding agency and the parent company/agency of
the expert.

Mocimboa da Praia                                   610,000

port of Pebane                                       20,000

Center (RTTC) in Chimoio, Manica Province           630,000
(vi)  Facilities for Road and Mechanical Training
Production Units (RTPU and MTPU)                    200,000
(vii) Facilities at Industrial Institute Maputo         1,700,000
(viii)  Contruction of houses for DNEP consultants    1,000,000

defined as a result of detailed structural investigations and design to
be financed by IDA under the project. The detailed scope of works for
the RTTC will be defined in a detailed design study also to be funded by
IDA under the project.
3.    While the marine contracts (i, ii and iii above) might be put into
one package, the training facilities (v and vi) into another, and the
houses in Maputo (viii, ix) into a third package it is most probable
that each of the items will be let under a different contract since the
decisioa makers on scope, optimal investment timing, and project
locations in each case are likely to be different.

SUMSMARY OF ECONOMIC ANALYSIS OF ROAD INVESTMENTS
1.     This annez provides a summary of the economic analysis for the
roads components. It is divided into two sections. The first provides
a eul-ary of the results of the feasibility studies of the major trunk
roads for which feasibility and detailed design are covered in this
project and actual rehabilitation will be covered in ROCS-IT. The
second section provides a brief analysis of a specific feeder road
rehabilitation, to elaborate on the generic analysis included in chapter
five.
Trunk Road Rehabilitation
2.     Of the eight trunk road sections to be rehabilitated in ROCS-II,
the feasibility studies have been coovpleted for seven. The results of
the economic analysis are shown below.
3.     The table shows the estimated financial costs of the chosen
option and the NPV and ERR in the base case and in two alternativess one
with 20Z higher construction costs and one with 20? lower initial
traffic levels.

Feeder Road Rehabilitation
4.     A key characteristic of the feeder road program is the maintaining of
sufficient flexibility to allow the program to be adapted to changes in the
security situation and changes in the pattern of agricultural rehabilitation. The
emphasis, therefore, has been not on the rigorous economic analysis of specific
stretches of road but rather on the formulation of selection criteria to ensure
that viable, high priority roads will be rehabilitated.
5.     It has already been indicated in chapter five that the economics of feeder
road rehabilitation is in general favorable. Economic benefits in terms of
savings in transportation costs, for a 'typical" 100-km stretch of road, have been
estimated in the FRP program document as shown belows
Product       Export         Savings per   Annual
Volume         Ton           Savings
Cashew             200t          $14.4          $2880
Copra              400t          $ 2.3          $ 920
Cotton             lOOt          $14.4          $1440
6.      In addition, a 25-km stretch has been selected for more detailed
analysis. As the table below shows, considering as benefits only the voc savings
due to the improved road's smoothness as a result of the rehabilitation (with
adequate future maintenance), the expected ERR is very high (almost 1751).
